fulfillment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng. It is assumed that the reader

==PAGE:5==
Preface
The present report is written as the last part of the Civil Engineering Master’s program at the
Technical University of Denmark, Department of Civil Engineering. The master thesis is a prod-
uct of various experimental works, data processing, analysis and simulations done in spring 2023
in the period 15th of February to the 15th of July. The report is conducted by Cecilie Hildebrandt
Troelsen, supervised by Carsten Rode and represents 30 ETCS points.
The scope of the master thesis is to assess the alternative insulation material hempcrete with focus
on hygrothermal performance and environmental performance.
i
==PAGE:6==
Abstract
The present study aims to investigate environmentally sustainable insulation materials for future
Danish industry. The bioorganic alternative insulation material, hempcrete, is scrutinized. The
report revolves around the following topics:
• Laboratory experiments obtaining material properties of hempcrete
• Simulations to conduct a Life Cycle Assessment of hempcrete
• Calculations and simulations to determine the hygrothermal performance of hempcrete
A Life Cycle Assessment is conducted because current studies are not up-to-date if considering
the new supplement to the Danish building regulation. In previous LCAs conducted, the functional
unit has also been problematic as a complete wall element is often used for comparison. Therefore,
an LCA of hempcrete was performed and compared to traditional insulation using a functional unit
specifically focused on insulation. The findings of the LCA displayed hempcrete as a promising
environmentally sustainable material, being useful in the Danish construction industry with a net
climate change output of 4.58E-01 kg CO2 eq.
To clarify if hempcrete is an alternative material concerning hydrothermal performance for the
Danish building industry, calculations and simulations are conducted. However, an issue arose
because the properties of hempcrete from Hemboo were only obtained through theoretical calcu-
lations. A comprehensive experimental work was conducted to determine the properties of the
hempcrete used. The properties obtained by experiments applied to insulation materials, while
the properties correspond to nowadays insulation materials such as mineral wool. Furthermore,
the thermal performance of the wall obliged with BR18 having a U-value of 0.189 W/(m2 ∗K).
However, simulation results obtained from the program Delphin 6.1 showed moisture problems.
With reservations that there is a rain screen solution available, hempcrete could be used as an al-
ternative insulation material in the Danish construction industry.
Keywords:
Hempcrete, insulation, hygrothermal properties, Life Cycle Assessment,
simulations, sustainability, lime, NHL
ii
==PAGE:7==
Resumé (Danish)
Dette projekt har til formål at undersøge miljømæssigt bæredygtige isoleringsmaterialer til den
fremtidige danske byggeindustri. Det bioorganiske alternative isoleringsmateriale, hampebeton,
bliver nøje undersøgt. Rapporten omhandler følgende emner:
• Laboratorieeksperimenter for at indhente materialeegenskaber for hampebeton
• Simuleringer for at udføre en livscyklusvurdering af hampebeton
• Beregninger og simuleringer for at bestemme hampebetons hygrotermiske ydeevne
En livscyklusvurdering udføres, fordi aktuelle undersøgelser ikke er opdaterede, hvis man tager
det nye supplement til bygningsreglementet i betragtning. I de forrige LCA’er udført er den funk-
tionelle enhed ligeledes problematisk, da der ofte benyttes et helt vægelement til sammenligning.
Derfor blev der udført en LCA af hampebeton og sammenlignet med traditionel isolering med en
funktionel enhed kun omfattende isolering. Resultaterne af LCA’en viser, at hampebeton er et
lovende miljømæssigt bæredygtigt materiale, der er anvendeligt i den danske byggeindustri med
et netto klimaændringsoutput på 4.58E-01 kg CO2 eq.
For at afklare om hampebeton er et alternativt materiale med hensyn til hygrotermisk ydeevne
i den danske byggeindustri, er der foretaget beregninger og simulationer. Dog opstod der et
problem, da egenskaberne for hampebeton fra Hemboo kun blev opnået gennem teoretiske bereg-
ninger. Der blev derfor udført omfattende eksperimentelt arbejde for at fastlægge egenskaberne for
den anvendte hampebeton. Egenskaberne, der blev opnået ved eksperimenterne, blev anvendt på
isoleringsmaterialer og svarer til egenskaberne for moderne isoleringsmaterialer som mineraluld.
Desuden viste de simulerede resultater fra programmet Delphin 6.1 fugtproblemer i væggen, der
overholdt BR18 og havde en U-værdi på 0.189 W/(m2 ∗K). Med forbehold for, at der findes en
regnskærm-løsning tilgængelig, kan hampebeton anvendes som et alternativt isoleringsmateriale i
den danske byggeindustri.
Nøgleord:
Hampe beton, isolering, hygrotermiske egenskaber, livscyklusvurdering,
simulationer, bæredygtighed, kalk, NHL
iii

Acknowledgment
To conduct this thesis in the last five months I want to express great gratitude and acknowledge-
ments to all that helped in the process. Without the providence of guidance and support throughout
the process, this project would have been doubtful. All contributions large and small are very much
appreciated and special thanks I give in particular to the following people.
Carsten Rode, Associated Professor, my thesis supervisor, for guidance throughout the thesis in
technical matters but also in structural matters of the experiments and the overall writing of the
thesis. Furthermore, for believing in the project.
Kurt Kielsgaard, Professor, for guidance and many talks about materials especially lime. Guid-
ance in conducting the experiments and instructing in procedure and possible challenges regarding
the experiments.
Rune Andersen, for LCA guidance, patience and encouraging persona and belief in the project.
Jacob Friis-Grigoncza, for experimental guidance and help with the construction of test speci-
mens for the cup method experiment.
Kristian Riskær Povlen, Team leader of the workshop and lab in building 119 and 130, for
helping with obstacles along the way in the moulding process and carbonation process of the
hempcrete.
Helena Rejndrup, CEO of Hemboo, for providing guidance in the moulding of hempcrete and
information about the product.
Hemboo, for providing materials such as lime and hemp to create hempcrete for laboratory exper-
iments.
Robert Svan and Claus Aagaard, lab technicians, for helping with creating the moulds for
the hempcrete test specimens and providence of carbon dioxide with associated regulators and
problem-solving in the CO2 chamber process.

CHAPTER 0. NOMENCLATURE
Page xiv of 186
==PAGE:19==
1
Introduction
The European Commission has set itself to be the first climate-neutral continent by 2050. The
European Green Deal is made to oblige to this aim, and the key figures are; climate neutral by
2050, 55 % less net greenhouse gas emissions by 2030, compared to 1990 levels [27]. To address
this major challenge, looking at the construction sector is relevant, as its emissions are significant.
Buildings are responsible for 39 % of global greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions, whereas mate-
rials and construction account for a staggering 11 % [42]. Considerable potential for reducing
GHG emissions is detected by using biogenic resources. Biogenic resources cover materials from
organic organisms like plants or animals, binding agents of carbon during growth and thus help
harvest CO2 from the atmosphere [19]. The biogenic resource hempcrete is investigated to under-
stand and elaborate if it contains desirable properties for modern insulation material. Desirable
insulation material for future construction would have a low environmental impact, low thermal
conductivity and high heat capacity. Also, hempcrete has desirable hygroscopic properties and a
possible positive influence on the indoor environment for people [51]. A growing interest in the
hempcrete material among researchers is detected when searching on "hempcrete" or "hemplime",
see figure 1.1. Despite the various articles and various topics, none elaborates on the hygrothermal
properties of exactly the hemp-binder combination and ratio used by Hemboo.
Figure 1.1: ScienceDirect articles per year findings by word "hempcrete" or "hemplime" for filter
"Research articles".
1
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1. BACKGROUND AND MOTIVATION
1.1
Background and Motivation
The motivation for this topic arose from the encounter with Helena Rejndrup from the company
Hemboo at a sustainability fair, "Building Green," in Copenhagen. Hemboo presented hempcrete
at the fair, and the conversation solidified the motivation. In this thesis, the motivation is to find
solutions for future construction that are practical, sustainable and usable in Danish construction.
Therefore, exploring an alternative insulation material such as hempcrete is interesting. The ma-
terial is straightforward, encompassing numerous advantageous properties, as mentioned below in
section 1.6 Materials.
1.2
Thesis statement
When introducing and implementing a "new" building material in the Danish construction indus-
try, it should be examined for its properties and performance in the danish climate. Hempcrete
from Hemboo and its known properties were based on theoretical calculations, with no physical
tests being conducted. This raised questions and a solid motivation to shed light on the subject
experimentally. Experimental work is comprehensive. Thereby the thesis was limited to the scruti-
nizing of thermal and moisture properties. These are essential to verify if the material can become
an alternative to traditional insulation materials. To investigate the performance, calculations and
simulations were conducted.
The problem with current LCAs for hempcrete is that they are not up to date. This thesis will
contribute to an updated LCA that complies with the new requirements stated in January 2023 by
the Danish building regulations. Current LCAs often only cover the phases "cradle-to-gate", lack-
ing essential life cycle stages to be complete due to previous legislation. Another issue regarding
existing LCAs is that they often compare entire wall components, which raises curiosity. A wall
can be constructed in significantly different ways, meaning various factors can influence the out-
come. In this thesis, the focus will solely be on hempcrete, using a functional unit that facilitates
the comparison across insulation instead.
These problems resulted in two hypotheses to be answered in this project:
Hypothesis 1: Hempcrete is an alternative insulation material with different moisture
properties than traditional insulation materials. Despite this, hempcrete is a use-
ful insulation material with useful hygrothermal properties able to implement in the
Danish construction industry.
Hypothesis 2: Hempcrete is an environmentally sustainable insulation material, and
it is useful in the Danish construction industry when trying to minimize the environ-
mental footprint.
Page 2 of 186
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.3. OBJECTIVE AND TASKS
1.3
Objective and tasks
This thesis aims to determine the hygrothermal properties of hempcrete and assess its environ-
mental impact by creating a Life Cycle Assessment of the product. Confer the City and Building
Instruction (SBI) 207 [45], four functional requirements are of particular significance when in-
vestigating alternative insulation materials in building components, as these insulation materials
consist of organic materials. The functional requirements concern fire safety, thermal insulation,
moisture conditions, and sound insulation. Additionally, environmental assessment and occupa-
tional health should also be considered. This thesis will scrutinise the subject of thermal insulation
and moisture conditions through laboratory experiments. The scope of this thesis is limited to these
two functional requirements and an environmental assessment.
1.3.1
Tasks and topics addressed to assess Hypothesis 1
Practical work (chapter 3). The design process for the experiments by creating moulds, casting
hempcrete, and learning how to work with the materials.
Experimental work (chapter 3). The hypothesis is focusing on assessing six hygrothermal prop-
erties. These are determined by limiting the task to five specific experiments which deliver relevant
properties. The six hygrothermal properties with the conducted experiments and associated meth-
ods are displayed below:
• Porosity by LBM2 method (section 3.3)
• Density by LBM2 method (section 3.3)
• Capillary suction by LBM1 method (section 3.4)
• Water vapour permeability by Cup method (section 3.5)
• Sorption Isotherm curve by Climate chamber method (section 3.6)
• Thermal conductivity by Guarded hot plate method (section 3.7)
Simulations and calculations (chapter 4). To examine the insulation material in a construction
context, a hempcrete wall will be used. Calculations of the thermal performance of the wall will
be conducted to assess if it complies with the BR18. Calculations of the moisture performance
will be carried out by stationary calculations of the Glaser method, delivering estimates of the
water vapour diffusion. To evaluate the performance with more realistic conditions, a dynamic
simulation is conducted in the program Delphin 6.1. These calculations and simulations are used
to assess the insulation material in the building industry. The criteria below should be evaluated
for the hempcrete wall:
• U-value < 0.3 W/(m2K)
• No accumulation of moisture content
• No risk of mould on inner surface or facade
• No exceeding of 20 mass% in the wall
• Minimum presence of 100 % relative humidity, only accepted when dry out occur
A comparison is performed in chapter 5 to verify the test results of the property findings with lit-
erature on hempcrete. Followed by comparison with other insulation materials to understand how
Page 3 of 186
==PAGE:22==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.3. OBJECTIVE AND TASKS
hempcrete differs from traditional insulation materials.
Literature work. Numerous study’s have been involved in the comparison of properties disclosed.
All numbers have been tried and verified by literature used in both practical and simulation work.
1.3.2
Tasks and topics addressed to assess Hypothesis 2
Simulation work (chapter 2). Design of the Life Cycle Assessment in the program OpenLCA
based on data and knowledge about hempcrete from the LCA database ecoinvent and the industry.
Standard DS/EN ISO14044 [9] states requirements for the execution of a LCA. The following
activities will be done:
• Goal and scope definition (section 2.1 and 2.2): Goal and scope is defined. Here the
substantiation of the performance of the LCA is mapped, outlining what the results are used
for, the method used and who will use the LCA.
• Life cycle inventory analysis (section 2.2): The life cycle of hempcrete is investigated
and outlined. This is used for proper data collection for all inputs, including materials and
processes.
• Life cycle impact assessment: (section 2.3) The impacts calculations by the ReCipe mid-
point (H) method in OpenLCA is done. Here impacts of reference materials, stone wool and
aerated concrete are calculated too and compared with hempcrete.
• Life cycle interpretation: (section 2.4) The assessment of the LCA is done.
To answer Hypothesis 2 statement, the tasks below are performed in chapter 2:
• Delivery on hotspot analysis of impact categories by the ReCipe Midpoint (H) method in
LCA of hempcrete, finding which life cycle stages that contribute the most to the environ-
mental impact of hempcrete
• Delivering an overall output of kg CO2 eq. for hempcrete
• Delivering an overall output of kg CO2 eq. for traditional insulation as mineral wool
• Comparison of traditional insulation to assess if it acts to minimize the carbon footprint.
• Assessment of the LCA by sensitivity analysis
• Assessment of the LCA by comparison with other LCA studies of hempcrete
Literature work. Numerous study’s have been involved in the comparison of existing LCAs and
in creating an overview of the manufacturing and disposal of hempcrete.

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.5. ALTERNATIVE INSULATION REQUIREMENTS
1.5
Alternative insulation requirements
Hempcrete is an alternative insulation material cast in situ, prefabricated in blocks, or sprayed onto
the construction. This alternative insulation can be used in various locations in construction, in-
cluding walls, roofs, and ground slabs, see Figure 1.3. The product hempcrete has been commonly
used in France over the last 40 years, and today it is widely used in England too. Hempcrete is
made from hemp shiv, a lime-based binder, and water and is suitable for energy renovation and
new buildings.
Figure 1.3: Use of hempcrete in buildings from the article "Hemp-lime composite for buildings
insulation: material properties and regulatory framework" [48]
The following functional requirements for alternative insulation in SBI 207 [45] will be briefly
elaborated on and compared with existing literature on hempcrete. Section 1.6 Materials, will
describe a more detailed description of the materials used and the appertaining properties.
Page 6 of 186
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.5. ALTERNATIVE INSULATION REQUIREMENTS
Fire safety requirements
The first functional requirement, fire safety, raises concerns, according to SBI 207 [45], as organic
materials are combustible. Organic materials often release relatively high heat and generate sig-
nificant smoke during a fire, compared to mineral wool insulation. The fire requirement in BR18
[53], chapter "Fire (§ 82 - § 158)", subsection 4.3, is as follows: "Insulation materials that meet
the requirements for at least material class B-s1, d0 [class A material] can be used without re-
strictions." This applies to Hemboo’s hempcrete according to Hemboo. Currently, this aspect is
being investigated at the Danish Institute of Fire and Security Technology´s (DBI) fire laboratory
to demonstrate and certify its fire properties.
Sound insulation requirements
According to SBI 207 [45], the functional requirement of sound insulation is often not a problem
with alternative insulation materials. BR18 aims to ensure a minimum level of sound insulation
between different rooms or areas within a building. The demand accounts for buildings where
you sleep; according to paragraph § 369, subsections 2, the acoustics must comply with class C.
The classification of air sound insulation differs between class F > 40 dB and class A > 63 dB for
the most common room types. Here the hempcrete complies with class C having a soundproofing
power of 57 dB when looking at a hemp-lime wall with a thickness between 300 to 400 mm [48].
This complies with the building regulation, and hempcrete is considered a sound insulation mate-
rial for current literature research.
Thermal insulation requirements
Thermal insulation is the third parameter and is crucial to avoid unnecessary energy consumption
by having well-insulated houses. In the building regulation under the chapter "Energy consump-
tion and climate impact (§ 250 - § 298)", § 257, the minimum requirements for the U-value of the
building envelope are specified; see U-values in table 1.1.
Building element
U-value [W/m^2K]
External walls and basement walls against the ground
0.3
Ground deck and basement floors against the ground
0.2
Ceiling and roof constructions
0.2
Table 1.1: Minimum demand of thermal insulation in BR18 [53]
According to Hemboo, the thermal conductivity is 0.08 W/m2K; generally, the thermal conduc-
tivity varies between 0.3 and 0.14 W/m2K depending on the density [48] [23]. This report will
consider how hempcrete is a viable insulation material, even if achieving the desired U-value
comes at the expense of a large amount of insulation mass. However, this significant mass, rang-
ing from 250 to 500 kg/m3 [48], also acts as a heat accumulator. It absorbs heat during the day
and releases it during the evening and night, contributing to the sensible regulation of the build-
ing’s energy consumption [55]. Besides the U-value, another parameter to verify and comply with
the regulations is having an energy demand not exceeding the regulations’ demands. The thermal
conductivity is tested in the laboratory in this thesis.
Page 7 of 186
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.5. ALTERNATIVE INSULATION REQUIREMENTS
Moisture requirements
Moisture management in construction is a parameter crucial for the indoor environment and the
performance of the building. Alternative insulation materials differ from conventional ones be-
cause they are made from biodegradable materials, which means they rely on the presence of
moisture. This fact is essential to remember when working with alternative insulation materi-
als; therefore, achieving moisture-technical correctness in design is critical. In the BR18 chapter
"Moisture and wet rooms (§ 334 - § 339)", there are no explicit restrictions, but general criteria,
are mentioned. Condensation, moisture, and water should not endanger people’s health or harm
building components. There shouldn’t be any problems with the growth of mould and fungus, es-
pecially those connected to thermal bridges, and there shouldn’t be any groundwater seepage from
rain or meltwater. To accommodate the moisture requirements in BR18, SBI 277 [28] will be
used to determine if the insulation material complies. This instruction helps assess the moisture-
technical function of the construction.
The calculation tools specified in SBI 277 [28] include manual calculations for stationary settings
and computer simulations for non-stationary heat and moisture situations. Hand calculations on
critical surface moisture levels to determine risk assessment of mould growth are investigated.
Hand calculations by the Glazer method are done to investigate water vapour diffusion, and lastly,
a simulation tool, Delphin 6.1, is used to determine the hygrothermal performance of a hempcrete
wall. The hand calculations provide a rough estimate of the moisture conditions, and the advanced
program Delphin provide more detailed results.
The Glaser method has limitations, especially when considering highly vapour-permeable mate-
rials like the one discussed in this thesis. It solely assesses moisture transport through vapour
diffusion and does not consider moisture transport via capillarity or sorption, which are crucial for
evaluating the likelihood of condensation in permeable materials. These parameters are important
to implement when evaluating the material because it affects the risk of condensation too. How-
ever, the Glaser method has been chosen due to its simplicity and ability to provide an estimate of
the material’s hygrothermal performance and the simulations will complement these findings and
more.
The simulation’s findings should be utilized to determine the practical importance of moisture
content for the assembly or construction. There are several ways to evaluate the results in the
instruction, see table 1.2.
Evaluation of simulations, SBI 277
- Check for accumulation or increase of moisture. It should reach periodical steady state condition.
- Compare computed moisture content with recognized limit values.
- Analyze the construction’s drying potential and its tolerance to moisture.
Table 1.2: Evaluation of simulation results, SBI 277 [28]
Different approaches are used to avoid condensation in the building envelope that might harm the
moisture-sensitive materials: vapour barrier, ventilation, or diffusion-open surfaces. According to
Page 8 of 186
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.6. MATERIALS
SBI 207, a water vapour resistance, Z, below 3 [GPa s m2/kg] indicates a vapour-permeable, be-
low 50 [GPa s m2/kg] indicates a vapour diffusion retarder, and above 50 [GPa s m2/kg] indicates
vapour barrier. This Z-value will be determined for hempcrete through experiments. A prerequi-
site for using constructions without a vapour barrier is that the interior plaster layer is airtight and
without holes and cracks through which water vapour can penetrate the wall.
Sustainability requirements
According to SBI 207 [45], an LCA should be conducted on alternative insulation material to
assess if it is sustainable and, thereby, a future product. In BR18 [53], the demands are stated in §
297 - § 298 "Climate impact". Here the climate impact should be calculated in kg CO2 equivalents
per m2 per year, also for 50 years. The climate requirement in BR18 does not include modules A4
and A5 compared to the sustainability class. The low emissions class is achieved when the climate
impact is equal to or less than 8.0 kg CO2 equivalents m2/year. Many literature studies are
already conducted on this matter, resulting in a positive response. This thesis will provide another
LCA on hempcrete to determine the product’s environmental impact by the new rules of BR18.
When determining if the product performs well, it is compared to other products complying with
the functional unit. Also, a hotspot analysis is conducted to determine critical impact categories.
1.6
Materials
In this thesis, biogenic materials such as lime and hemp are used to produce the alternative insula-
tion material, hempcrete, also known as hemp-lime, lime-hemp, or hemp concrete. The following
section presents the materials used and investigated in this thesis.
1.6.1
Lime
The lime-based binder used is the product Batichanvre from the producer Saint-Astier. It is a
Natural Hydraulic Lime (NHL) from burned limestone completely free of foreign bodies such as
sulfate, clay, iron, and aluminates. NHL-3.5 is used in this thesis, and the number indicates that
it is a moderately strong lime with a compressive strength of 3.5 MPa. To harden hydraulic lime,
air and water must be present because the NHL contains air lime and some hydraulic components
generated during burning. The air lime needs air to carbonate, and the other components need
water for hydration [41]. The extraction of lime from burned limestone is done to achieve the
desired workability. The calcium carbonate in the lime undergoes different processes shown in the
lime circle figure 1.4 below.
Page 9 of 186
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.6. MATERIALS
Figure 1.4: Lime circle illustrating the calcium carbonate process when used in the industry
The calcium carbonate processes with formulas are described in three steps calcination, lime slak-
ing, and carbonation. By calcination, the carbon dioxide gasses off when the limestone is heated,
and calcium oxide is left. The lime re-gains its desired natural properties when the product is
processed. The formula for calcination is described in equation 1.1.
CaCO3 →CaO + CO2
(1.1)
After this, the hydrated lime is created by mixing the burnt lime with water. This process is named
lime slaking, and here carbonic acid is formed, see equation 1.2.
CaO + H2O →Ca(OH)2
(1.2)
Lastly, the carbonic acid and the hydrated lime reacts, and the carbonation occurs. This process
returns the original calcium carbonate, and water is expelled, see equation 1.3.
CO2 + H2O →H2CO3andCa(OH)2 + H2CO3 + 2H2O
(1.3)
The carbonation changes the hydrated lime back to limestone, and therefor the hardness of the
material increases. The flexibility of lime mortar, which permits minor distortion without break-
ing, and its unique open-porosity, which results in high permeability for vapour but not for liquid
water, are its two key building-related properties [29]. Other impacts are the pH value which
decreases since the calcium carbonate is a neutral compound. The weight will change because
the calcium carbonate is heavier. The structure is compact, too, leaving the porous smaller after
carbonation. However, the smaller pores are beneficial when looking at the capillary forces. They
become stronger with small pores, thereby allowing the suction of water more effectively. The
water permeability of the material will become higher too. This change of porous structure, where
there are larger capillaries because of the small cubic crystals of the denser CaCO3, affects the
suction and permeability of the material [46].
Page 10 of 186
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.6. MATERIALS

Table 1.3: Chemical composition of Batichanvre NHL-3,5 [13]
*CAS number: Chemical Abstract Service and describes a unique numeric identifier of a chemical.
Figure 1.5: Batichanvre NHL-3.5 from St. Astier
In general, the overall composition is roughly consisting of fillers such as calcium carbonate and
silica. Else the components are air lime, calcium dihydroxide, and hydraulic lime, calcium silicate,
other binders and additives in Batichanvre.
Calcium Dihydroxide is the air lime component and will harden by carbonation. This process
can take years with natural carbonation, where the air concentration is about 400 ppm. In the
Batichanvre lime, there is about 10-20 % of Calcium Dihydroxide and by using the CO2 chamber
the process will be enhanced.
The calcium silicate of 5-20 % will harden by water. The calcium silica, also known as clinker
mineral, is in chemical cementation notation called C2S and the hardening process is illustrated
below in figure 1.6, being the 2CaO*SiO2. The curve shows that the test specimens will not harden
concerning the C2S part of the lime because it takes about one year, and enough water has to be
present [46].
Page 11 of 186
==PAGE:30==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.6. MATERIALS
Figure 1.6: Cement strength from [46]
The other components as calcium carbonate 30-60 % and silica, crystalline 10-50 % are so-called
fillers. Fillers help make the lime act as a binder in the hempcrete. The calcium carbonate is
burned at lower temperatures meaning not all CO2 is extracted from it, and thereby the need for
carbonation is not necessary. Silicate does not react with any other chemical and acts as a filler, as
mentioned earlier. Technical properties of Batichanvre NHL-3.5 in tabel 1.4.

1.6.2
Hemp
The hemp shives used in the samples are from BAFA Neu GmbH Bernd Frank and are Planete
Chanvre’s shives. It is sold by the company Tradical® which specializes in air lime, and the
product is called Chanvribat. The hemp known for industrial purposes has a Tetrahydrocannabinol
(THC) level, a psychoactive substance, of less than 0.2 %. The hemp production in Europe is about
50,000 hectares, and mainly 37 % is produced in France according to [12].
Figure 1.7: Chanvribat hemp from Tradical
Page 12 of 186

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.6. MATERIALS
The composition of hemp consists of mostly cellulose, hemicellulose, and lignin, as shown in table
1.5. The cellulose is the most significant natural polymer, and the primary function of cellulose
is to provide strength to the plant. It is a homogenous linear polymer consisting of glucose units.
The hemicellulose will be a binder between cellulose and lignin. Hemicellulose consists of various
sugars, and the structure is heterogeneous and branched, making the hemicellulose more accessible
to water. Hemicellulose is a significant contributor to the property of hygroscopicity in hemp.
Lastly, lignin is a component giving strength to the plant and protects it against pathogens [40].

Page 13 of 186
==PAGE:32==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.6. MATERIALS
The hemp material in hempcrete is the major contributor to the hygroscopic properties. A param-
eter affecting the end product of hempcrete is the amount of dust in the hemp shive production.
The dust can have an impact on structural integrity because it soaks a lot of water. If there is too
much dust present in the mix, the amount of water for the lime binder will be too low and thereby
causing the binder to fail [51]. A crucial property of hemp shives is thereby the amount of dust
present. Also, the hemp shives should be kept dry at a maximum of 20 % humidity to ensure that
there won’t be any excess water entering the hempcrete mix. And if the hemp shives are exposed
to moisture for a longer period it will start rotting [51]. The Chanvribats also have a maximum
lifetime of one year in the stored bags before use. Below in figure 1.6 the properties of hemp used
in the thesis are shown.

1.6.3
Hempcrete
Hempcrete is made from hemp shiv, the woody part of the plant stem, a lime-based binder and
water. The lime binder act as a connective matrix between the hemp shives and gives the hempcrete
the properties of fire protection and fungal protection with a high pH value.

CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.7. LITERATURE RESEARCH
As seen in table 1.7, the insulation material does not provide a significant load-bearing strength.
Hempcrete can only support its own weight, and this composite material, like other cast materials,
needs to properly harden and cure for approximately four to eight weeks [47]. However, the curing
time varies greatly depending on the thickness of the insulation. Traditionally, hempcrete walls
are built by having a load-bearing timber frame surrounded by hempcrete that is cast in situ. The
formwork can be removed after about 24 hours, and then the curing period occurs as mentioned.
Prefabricated hempcrete components can also be made, as practised by Hemboo, and hempcrete
can also be used for energy renovation, where it is often sprayed on. This alternative insulation
material has many qualities, which are mentioned below:
• Fireproof: Because hemp fibres contain a significant amount of silica, hempcrete is naturally
fire-resistant. It improves building safety and do not release harmful gases when exposed to
fire [51]. According to Danish building regulations, hempcrete is a class A material when
being in fire class B-s1-d0 and can be used without restrictions in construction.
• Sound insulation: Hempcrete absorbs sound waves, reducing noise transmission between
rooms or from external sources, and have a soundproofing power of 57 dB [48], which
complies with Danish building regulation.
• Insulation properties: According to Hemboo, the thermal conductivity is 0.08 W/m2K of
hempcrete, and generally, the thermal conductivity varies between 0.3 and 0.9 W/m2K de-
pending on the density [48]. Besides, hempcrete has a great thermal mass compared to
traditional insulation materials such as glass whole, and thereby, it acts as a heat accumula-
tor too [55].
• Moisture properties: The hygroscopic material hempcrete allows moisture to travel through
the construction, making the indoor environment attractive, while the moisture buffer effect
regulates the moisture content. The good vapour permeability reduces mould and moisture
issues [51].
• pH value: The lime binder in the hempcrete has a high basic pH value and thereby prevents
the growth of pests [51].
• Sustainable: It is considered an environmentally friendly alternative to traditional insula-
tion materials [39] [17] because of the lime and hemp acting as carbon sinks and the hemp,
which grows quickly and requires minimal pesticides or herbicides. Also, the material is
considered a renewable material and easy to recycle [48]. Another aspect comes into ac-
count when investigating embodied carbon. Here lime-based binders have less embodied
carbon when compared to cement, and this is due to the fact of different manufacturing
processes. Limestone is burned at lower temperatures, around 900 to 1000 oC [51], while
cement is manufactured at 1500 degrees Celcius [46]. The energy-intensive process releases
a lot of greenhouse gases into the atmosphere.
• Durability: The lifespan of hempcrete is considered 100 years or more when adequately
conducted. [51].
1.7
Literature research
1.7.1
LCA studies of hempcrete
History of LCA
The Danish Sustainability Class [16] defines the goals for the Danish industry. BR18 refers to the
Page 15 of 186
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.7. LITERATURE RESEARCH
class stating that all new structures must undergo a life-cycle assessment (LCA), a fundamental
requirement. The demands in BR18 is the output climate impact calculated in kg CO2 equiva-
lents per m2 per year, for a 50-year period, see 1.5 Alternative insulation requirements. Data from
environmental product declarations (EPDs) or generic data is utilized to determine the climatic
effect of products. Old EPDs were developed according to a standard EN15978+A1, which ex-
pired in June 2023. EPDs prepared according to the old standard are generally not required to
declare disposal information (C1-C4). However, in EPDs prepared according to the new standard
EN15804+A2 [11], the disposal stages (C1-C4) are included and should be declared. Before, the
cradle-to-gate concept was the prior demand for EPDs. More is fortunately added today, trying to
ensure the most accurate climate impact. The elaboration of the history of quantifying the envi-
ronmental impact is done to understand what reorientates old and new LCAs.
LCA’s of hempcrete
Due to its ability to utilise renewable raw resources and capture carbon dioxide through photosyn-
thesis, bio-based building materials have the potential to be a more environmentally friendly alter-
native to conventional mineral construction materials. The industrial hemp plant used in hempcrete
has a huge potential regarding renewable materials. Hemp has a short growth cycle without using
pesticides or irrigation, making it a very easy and producible crop [51]. Through photosynthesis
and carbonation of the lime-based binder, hempcrete acts somehow as a renewable resource and
as a sequester of CO2 [51].
Current hempcrete LCA studies, [39], [26], [17], [52], [49] and [33] show that hempcrete has
the potential to be carbon negative throughout its life cycle and to store more Greenhouse gas
(GHG) than it emits. In these research articles, a high hemp shiv CO2 sequestration factor, a
high degree of binder carbonation, and an underdeveloped or omitted end-of-life phase are key
elements leading to a negative GHG output. In tabel, 1.10 summarization of some studies is
shown and it is illustrated how the key elements mentioned are leading to good environmental
climate performance.
Page 16 of 186
==PAGE:35==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.7. LITERATURE RESEARCH

publications did not include the information.

==PAGE:36==
2
Life cycle assessment
The Life Cycle Assessment has been conducted, as mentioned, to analyse and answer hypothesis
number 2, which was addressed in section 1.2 and subsection 1.3.2. In this chapter, a Life Cy-
cle Assessment (LCA) follows guidelines from the standard ISO 14044 [8]. The program used
to conduct the LCA is OpenLCA. The subsections involve goal and scope definition, Life cycle
inventory analysis, Life cycle impact assessment (LCIA), Interpretation phase, Completeness and
consistency check, Sensitivity analysis, and a critical view. Nowadays, it is claimed that con-
structions with hempcrete are environmentally sustainable based on the materials acting as carbon
sinks. Hemp shives are a natural material absorbing carbon through photosynthesis while growing.
The lime absorbs carbon in the carbonation process. Also, hempcrete is often used with a struc-
tural framework of timber, which acts as a carbon sink under photosynthesis. This claim is being
investigated further in the following sections to scrutinise if hempcrete has a good performance
regarding the environmental impact and how it performs when compared to traditional insulation
materials.
2.1
Goal definition
Intended applications of the results
This study aims to perform an LCA of hempcrete for external use. It is performed to investigate
environmental hot spots in the life cycle processes and to create comparability with other insulation
materials complying with the same functional unit. In this study, all impact categories provided by
ReCipe Midpoint (H) are in focus but the climate change impact is the main delivery. The results
are meant to broaden one’s horizon for the environmental impact of the product hempcrete.
Limitations due to methodological choices
As the ReCipe Midpoint (H) method is used for the LCIA some limitations are present. Here
only the impact categories covered by this method are represented in this report. The LCA can
be used to compare identical impact categories, thereby, no overall equal impact unit covering all
categories at once will be delivered. This LCA will deliver multiple comparisons where the impact
category "Climate change" act as comparative figures while this category is the current factor valid
for the Danish Building regulation.
18
==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
Reasons for carrying out the study and decision context
To meet the building regulations and, thereby the future environmental building industry, an alter-
native insulation product, hempcrete is investigated for its environmental footprint. This study is
conducted to determine if hempcrete is environmentally sustainable and if the outcome is positive
and offers an environmentally friendly insulation material compared to other insulation materials.
It is created to determine whether or not hempcrete should be supported in the industry and to
attract environmentally conscious consumers. This LCA study might not lead to any structural
changes so far. It would be classified as a "Situation A Micro level decision support". Here the
study might lead to limited changes and maybe identify the pros and cons of hempcrete, leading
to less or more product use.
Target audience
The LCA’s primary target audience is the company Hemboo, consumers, the industry, environmental-
and civil engineers and developers. In general, the target is all authorities involved in hempcrete
somehow. How hempcrete performs, should be clear to all, unimportant a good or bad outcome.
Comparative studies to be disclosed to the public
This study is meant to be disclosed to the public, while it is for external use.
Commissioner of the study and other influential actors
The commissioner of this LCA is the firm Hemboo.
2.2
Scope definition
Deliverables
The LCA conducted is a Cradle to Grave LCA; below is an elaboration of deliverables.
• Sensitivity analysis
A sensitivity analysis was carried out to determine how sensitive the LCA findings are con-
cerning changes in input parameters. This was done by computing normalized sensitivity
coefficients for all input parameters, with each parameter being disturbed with an increase
of 10 %. The formula 2.1 below was used to find the sensitivity coefficient, Scoef .
Scoef =
∆IS
IS0
∆ak
ak,0
[−]
(2.1)
Where ak,0 is the original parameter value and ak is the difference between the increased
parameter value and original parameter value, IS0 IS0 is the initial impact score and ∆IS
is the difference between the impact score based on the increased parameter value and the
initial impact score.
• Uncertainty analysis
Uncertainty analysis of the LCA outcome was propagated using Monte Carlo simulations to
reflect uncertainty in the input parameters and the background eco-invent processes. With
10,000 iterations, the Monte Carlo simulation was run in the program OpenLCA.
Page 19 of 186
==PAGE:38==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
• Hot spot analysis
A hot spot analysis is conducted to identify which processes in the life cycle that have the
greatest impact. The analysis is simple and performed on the processes or stages that one
wants to identify contributors for the greatest impact.
Object of the assessment
The functional unit is chosen with respect to the definition given by the standard ISO 14040 [8].
To have comparability between the alternative insulation material hempcrete and a traditional in-
sulation material a regular U-value of 0.2 W/m2K is chosen. This demand is based on the Danish
building regulation for the envelope of a building. Minimum demand for the construction accord-
ing to the Danish building regulation [53] is shown in the table 1.1 in the introduction.
Functional unit:
1 m2 of insulation of a maximum U-value of 0.2 W/m2K for a reference
lifetime of 50 years.
The benchmark of a U-value of 0.2 involves hempcrete insulation being 40 cm thick compared
with traditional stone wool insulation, which only needs a thickness of 18,5 cm to comply with
the U-value. It is chosen not to look at the whole constructed wall, roof or floor partly because
of the big variations there might be regarding construction possibilities. As a result, the output of
the LCA would be difficult to interpret for comparing insulation materials if whole elements were
simulated. Obligatory and positioning properties of hempcrete is listed below in 2.1.

The reference flow of this LCA is defined as a duration of 50 years based on the demand from the
Danish sustainability class and in accordance with the standard DS/EN15978:2012. In the industry
the lifetime is considered to be 100 years [51], [39], [49], despite the fact that the building material
has not been on the market for long yet. Only time will tell what the lifespan of hempcrete is.

==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
System boundaries and completeness requirements
The system boundaries of hempcrete are consisting of a separation of the background and fore-
ground systems. The background system is all the raw materials and production of these to be-
come materials used in hempcrete. Here lime is recovered from limestone and hemp is produced
by agriculture and harvested. The data used is obtained from the eco-invent database containing
often average industry data in specific nations. The foreground system consists of the specific
products Batichanvre, Chanvribat and water. These are used in the hempcrete produced by Hem-
boo and this system is modelled using the eco-invent database, even though it would have been
preferred to use primary data.
Representativeness of Life Cycle Inventory (LCI) data
Carbonation is not an immediate effect and takes many years, therefore it is difficult to know
which values should be used when analysing the environmental impacts of the binder. The rate of
carbonation is highly variable as, although currently unknown, the thickness, type and porosity of
the render affects the diffusion of CO2 into the hemp-lime. Predominant weather conditions also
vary the rate of carbonation, therefore, two identical walls built in different countries will almost
certainly carbonate differently. In this thesis, 100 % carbonation is assumed. A calculation has
been conducted and presented later to accommodate the carbonation in the use phase.
Preparation of the basis for the impact assessment
The open-source software for life cycle assessments, OpenLCA, is used to model the Life Cycle
Inventory Analysis (LCIA). The modelling is based on the LCIA method, ReCiPe Midpoint (H),
and the indicators concentrated on the environmental issues shown below. These impact categories
below are included in the LCIA modelling of the hempcrete.

2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
Data collection
The life cycle inventories were gathered from Hemboo regarding the foreground system. Here
Hemboo delivered the processes involved the production of hempcrete and the bill of materials.
The background system was uneasy to get data from and the database eco-invent was here used
to collect the needed information for the harvesting of hempcrete and the mining of lime amongst
other things. All collected data is extracted from OpenLCA and represented in Appendix B.
System model per life cycle stage
In this section, an overview of significant assumptions, data collection and handling of these as
well as details of the system modelling are presented. The different life cycle stages and the asso-
ciated assumptions are displayed below.
Material stage
During growth, hemp is considered a carbon sink, while it sequesters the carbon by photosyn-
thesis. The natural hydraulic lime is considered to release carbon during the calcination of the
limestone. The NHL 3.5 used in this hempcrete product is Batichanvre and it consists of different
components. These, and in general materials used for hempcrete, were investigated and data from
eco-invent matching as good as possible were chosen. Down below in figure 2.3 in section 1.6, the
assumptions made for the materials are displayed. In general, the product Batichanvre had a range
for the amount of each component being present, see figure 1.3. Thereby, a decision has been
made as to how much approx. each component will constitute the product. Therefore, a parame-
ter has also been introduced for each component to accommodate uncertainty. The production of
hemp and lime takes place in France hence all processes preferably would be in this country or
nearby.
Page 24 of 186
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
Calcium Oxide
Calcium oxide is produced in the process of producing lime. Only
below 1 % is present in Batichanvre and thereby assumed that it
will form part of the quicklime process.
Calcium Dihydroxide
The input "Quicklime, milled, packed" is chosen for calcium di-
hydroxide and is called this in everyday speech. The best provider
was Switzerland. The component is set to 15 % value with an un-
certainty of min. 10 % and max. of 20 %.
Calcium Carbonate
The input "calcium carbonate, precipitated" is chosen, and the
provider is representative of Europe. Precipitated refers to the fact
that it is industrially produced. This is not ideal, because the prod-
uct from Saint Astier is a natural hydraulic lime product and on
the website, it is stated that the production is from the burning and
slaking of a pure chalky limestone with siliceous content and no
other additions are made. So the chosen input is the best substitute
because of the lack of other possible inputs in OpenLCA but is not
totally representative. The component is set to 40 % value with an
uncertainty of min. 30 % and max. of 60 %.
Calcium Silicate
The input "Clinker" is chosen because it is consistent with the
properties of calcium silicate. Unfortunately, the only provider
was Brazil and thereby geographically the consistency is less pre-
cise. Though is the production of Portland cement clinker a com-
mon and well-established production, thereby assuming the data is
representative. The component is set to a 5 % value with an uncer-
tainty of min. 5 % and max. of 20 %.
Aluminium Oxide
There is a consequent minimal presence of aluminium oxide
(Al2O3) when producing NHL, and thereby it is assumed that the
Al2O3 is presented in the other components.
Silica, Crystaline
The input "silica, sand production" is chosen for the production of
Silica. The provider is Germany and is considered of good repre-
sentativeness. The component is set to 25 % value with an uncer-
tainty of min. 10 % and max. of 50 %.
Hydraulic Binder
The input "Hydralic lime" produced in Switzerland represents the
component hydraulic binder. The component is set to 15 % value
with an uncertainty of min. 5 % and max. of 20 %.
Hemp shives
The input "sunn hemp production" is chosen and the location is
Rest of World (RoW). The data set is from an Indian data set and
was chosen for lack of better choices in OpenLCA.
Table 2.3: Life Cycle Inventory assumptions and choices, material stage
Manufacturing stage
When constructing the hempcrete on site, the need for electricity for mixing occurs. The unit pro-
cess "Electricity, medium voltage" mix from Denmark, was chosen. The amount of power needed
for a machinery called a pan mixer of 800 litres, often used for mixing hempcrete [51], is esti-
mated. The input in the LCA should consist of the electricity used for 1 kg produced hempcrete.
Page 25 of 186
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
The pan mixer is running for 10 minutes when mixing, and it is assumed that 0.0225 MJ is used.
An approx. calculation based on a pan mixer found on Toolster.com. The motor uses 15 kW, which
is 2.5 kWh for 10 minutes, and it is equal to 9 MJ. The pan mixer contains 400 L of hempcrete,
and thereby the final electricity needed for 1 kg of hempcrete is 0.0225 MJ. Production and cast-
ing of hempcrete on site by man-power do not require any further inputs, and it is thereby not a
comprehensive phase regarding the environmental impact. Under construction on site waste of
materials could vary depending on good and bad practices. Thereby a waste is considered as an
output, having 0 % for the best case, in general, 5 % and 10 % waste scenario for the worst case
of construction practice on site.
Use stage
The hydraulic lime is carbonating and thereby considered a carbon sink in this stage. Eventually,
full carbonation is reached, and no more carbon can be sequestered. Thereby 100% carbonation
is assumed for the use phase for the calcium hydroxide. In total, 0.03923 kg CO2 is considered
absorbed from the atmosphere over a lifetime of 50 years for 1 kg of hempcrete. This is calcu-
lated by considering the amount of calcium hydroxide of 0.04999 kg/kg hempcrete. The mass is
converted into moles using the lime molar mass, 56.06 g/mol. The mass of 0.04999 kg divided
by 56.08 g/mol equals 0.891 mol. It is known that the reaction is 1:1 between calcium oxide and
carbon dioxide, concluding that 0.891 mol of carbon dioxide will be used. To insert in OpenLCA,
a conversion into kilo grams have been conducted. Carbon dioxide has a molar mass of 44.01
g/mol. Thereby the mass of carbon dioxide is approx. 0.03923 kg.
The construction of a hempcrete wall is considered almost maintenance-free. The indoor and
outdoor coating of lime plaster for protection of the wall will need maintenance. This is estimated
to be every 50 years for the indoor lime plaster because the wear is less extensive than the outer
plaster. The outer lime plaster is estimated to be maintained every 33 years [52]. The lime plaster
is not part of the LCA conducted in this study, but to understand the use stage of a hempcrete wall,
the clarification of the wall was conducted.
End of Life stage and Beyond the Building Life cycle stage
In general, most actors who speak in favour of progress for hempcrete claim that the product can
be recycled and reused. Frequently the analogy is that the product is a non-toxic material and
thereby does no harm. And because it is entirely biodegradable the material is recyclable easily
[51]. In literature, it is not easy to find examples of the end of life practices in real life. This might
have something to do with the fact that the material is somewhat novel material to the industry
when created in the 1980s. The lifespan is expected to be 100 years in real life, as mentioned
earlier, and thereby far from all the buildings constructed in hempcrete have reached their end of
life. The last phase is, in many cases, not conducted in the existing LCA studies, and if they are
implemented, the material is considered waste in a landfill. In this thesis, different scenarios are
considered for the hempcrete; 1) landfill and 2) composting.
A third option is mentioned but not simulated, recycling. Today, it is not allowed to implement
a recycling stage in an LCA. All three scenarios are outlined below, and the recycling phase is
considered to be in the "Beyond the Building Life cycle stage" and only mentioned to be aware of
the potential.
Page 26 of 186
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.2. SCOPE DEFINITION
1) Landfill
When hempcrete is deposited at the landfill, a prior deconstruction of the building takes place.
This is stage C1, and it will require electricity when mechanically deconstructing wall, roof or
floor elements of hempcrete and wood. Afterwards, the transport, stage C2, is assumed to consist
of 20 km from the building site to the landfill. Lastly, the disposal, stage C4, takes place. No
decomposition of the hempcrete is considered [52], and there are no greenhouse gas emissions.
The assumption is that the lime binder encases the hemp shives, making them inert. The input
"sanitary landfill" is chosen in OpenLCA.
2) Composting
Here it is considered that the hemp shives are degradable because the material is bioorganic. The
process consists of the same deconstruction, stage C1, as in the landfill scenario. Though the ap-
proach is a bit different, the material is crushed to minor pieces after the overall deconstruction of
elements to undergo biodegradation [17]. Two studies [43] and [44] investigate the biodegradation
of hempcrete and what parameters must be present to observe mould growth. Factors such as high
humidity are crucial, as well as a high temperature for a longer period. Also, another parameter
that applies is the decrease of the pH value as carbonation occurs. The study showed that a pH
above 10 would prevent the moulding process from even beginning. Thereby it is crucial to crush
the hempcrete into minor pieces to make the exposed area as big as possible to increase the con-
tact with ambient conditions. When hempcrete undergoes biodegradation, the release of CO2 and
methane (CH4) occurs at the end of life.
Recycling D-phase
Overall the general way to recycle hempcrete is to crush it and re-mix it with a freshly mixed
hempcrete blend. Here screed bases can be created as well as bedding mortar, sand plasters and
insulation material [48]. It is also proven useful as an aggregate for construction blocks, the ratio
is then 90 % to blocks and 10 % to waste [17]. Another recycling method of hempcrete is to use
the higher pH in the crumbled and grinded hempcrete to provide fertilizer when spread across the
field [48]. Often a high pH is not desired in soil, but if the soil is having a too acidic environment,
this crumbled hempcrete might help regulate it.
Transport
The main harvest of hemp in Europe is in France, where also Chanvribats er produced. The overall
main distance is between Denmark, the building site, and France, the producer. It is assumed that
the building site will be located in the middle of Jutland. The distance is approx. 1300 kilometres,
and the input chosen is "Transport, freight, lorry 16-32 metric ton, EURO5". Uncertainty of ±
5 % is added as a parameter. Furthermore, smaller transportation was implemented, from the
extraction of limestone to the manufacturing of lime and harvesting of hemp and manufacturing
of hemp shives. Here a total of 120 km were assumed to represent this transport based on the
research of the process.
LCI results
The documentation of the LCI results is shown in appendix B and is a list of quantified elementary
flow divided into resources and emissions.

2.3. LIFE CYCLE IMPACT ASSESSMENT
2.3
Life Cycle Impact Assessment
The composing of LCIA is done with the selection of impact categories, classification, and charac-
terization. The impact categories are prepared for and shown earlier in table 2.2 using the ReCiPe
midpoint (H) method. Further, the classification of elementary flows of the LCI into the afore-
mentioned categories is conducted to map how the flows will contribute. The results of the life
cycle impacts of the alternative insulation material hempcrete were computed and displayed in the
following.
Figure 2.2: Life Cycle stages of Hempcrete
In the life cycle of hempcrete, mainly three stages are ongoing respectively the product stage A1
to A3 where the component is produced and hemp and NHL are manufactured. subsequently,
the hemp and NHL are transported to the building site where it too is being constructed into
an alternative insulation material used in walls, roofs and floors. This stage characterized the
process stage A4 to A5 and as illustrated in the figure only use stage B1 occurs, while hempcrete
is maintenance-free. Lastly, the end-of-life stage C1 to C4 takes place. The hempcrete element of
the house is deconstructed and either transported to a landfill or a composting facility.
2.3.1
Characterized impact profile
The ReCipe Midpoint [H] method was used to characterize the profile of the hempcrete regarding
impact categories. The impact categories in table 2.4 down below summarizes the life-cycle stages
considered for hempcrete as explained in figure 2.2. A Heatmap has been added to the results in
appendix B to generate an overview of the smaller impacts of the environment (green) and the
bigger impacts of the environment (red). The color comparison is only between stages in the
same impact category, because the categories have different units and are thereby not comparable
crosswise. The total composting is compared with the total landfill in the end.

The overall uncertainty of the LCA outcome was propagated using Monte Carlo simulation to
reflect uncertainty in the input parameters as well as the background ecoinvent processes. With
10,000 iterations, the Monte Carlo simulation was run.
In figure 2.6 generally, the C1-C4, End of Life stage, appears to be the lowest, except for two
categories: "Urban land occupation" and "Ozone depletion," if hempcrete is landfilled. However,
the uncertainty analysis using Monte Carlo shows that there are scenarios where a comparison
of the best case for "Total hempcrete (by composting)" with the worst case for "Total hempcrete
(by landfill)" would yield the opposite conclusion. In this case, a landfill would be the worst-
performing option.
Sensitivity analysis of Hempcrete
In total 10 parameters were used in the sensitivity analysis; "CalciumCar", "Clinker", "Hempcon",
"HydraulicLime", "Limecon", "Quicklime", "Silica", "transport", "Waste", and "quicklime" (in
B1-B7). How the analysis is conducted is explained in section Scope definition subsection Deliv-
erables. In Appendix B section B.2 a table of the sensitivity coefficients is shown. A heatmap has
been added to the results in the table to generate an overview of the smaller coefficients (green)
and the bigger coefficients (red). Some results were very sensitive in general the parameter Calci-
umCar, Hempcon, Limecon, and transport.
Knowing from the hot spot analysis that lime was a primary influence on several impact cate-
gories, it makes sense that a change in the lime concentration (Limecon) will have an impact on
the results. This also applies to calcium carbonate (CalciumCar), which is a significant component
at 40 % in NHL 3.5, thus these two parameters partially express the same thing. For hempcrete
concentration (hempcon), there is high sensitivity regarding the impact categories of "Water de-
pletion" and "agricultural land occupation." Lastly, the transport parameter is highly sensitive in
four categories and moderately sensitive in others, highlighting once again the significant role of
transport as an uncertainty factor in the overall LCA assessment.

2.4
Life Cycle interpretation
Overall, the performance of hempcrete showed a net positive result for all impact categories, see
2.4. Here the net positive results for GHG emissions were 4.58E-01 kg CO2-Eq for EoL being
landfill. A1-A3, the production stage, is the largest contributor to the overall total performance of
the LCA across all impact categories except for fossil depletion and urban land occupation, here
A4 had the highest contribution. Generally, A4, transport to construction, was the second largest
contributor in all categories. The categories that were most affected were as follows, here A1-A3
accounted for a percentage of 98% Agricultural land occupation, 45% Climate change, 40% Fossil
depletion, 94% Freshwater ecotoxicity, 73% Human toxicity, 81% Ionising radiation, 89% Marine
ecotoxicity, 98 % Metal depletion, 29 % Urban land occupation and 97 % Water depletion. Lime
production had the greatest impact on the results in categories A1-A3, except for two categories
"Agricultural land occupation" and "Water depletion", where hemp production accounted for the
largest share. This also makes sense as these categories are involved in the production of crops.
Page 34 of 186
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.4. LIFE CYCLE INTERPRETATION
When conducting the uncertainty analysis by Monte Carlo simulation the output showed, see fig-
ure 2.6, that variation definitely could occur. The uncertainty was significant, to the extent that
even though it was generally shown that composting performed worse than landfill, the outcome
could be reversed if one considered the lower margin for composting compared to landfill. This
analysis demonstrates that numerous parameters have an influence, and if one considers the "best
case" scenario, it can yield a much better result than the "worst case" scenario. One example could
be the GHG emission category, here the immediate results show that the LCA with EoL of Land-
fill performs the best. However, if we instead consider the highest possible output for landfill and
the lowest possible output for composting, it can be observed that composting will perform 20%
better than landfill. Most categories had a difference of approx. 25% and several had as high as
approx. 50 % difference, when comparing best and worst case.
To investigate what affects the output and to evaluate the output, a sensitivity analysis was con-
ducted, see appendix B. The sensitivity analysis indicated that the results were very sensitive to
parameters such as lime ratio, the component "Calcium carbonate" in the Batichanvre product, the
hemp shive ratio and the transport. The LCA of this study is less robust concerning these input
parameters.
As BR18 only assesses the climate footprint of buildings based on GHG emissions, the following
will address this impact category.
Figure 2.9: Comparison of hempcrete stages for the impact score Climate change
In the production phase, hempcrete accounts for 55 % of which lime production accounts for 82
% of the GHG emissions. Transport, A4, of hemp and lime from France to Denmark greatly con-
tributes due to the long distance involved of 1300 km. This resulted in a 49 % contribution to the
GHG emission for the entire LCA of hempcrete. The only net negative stage is B1-B7, the use
stage, providing a -6% contribution due to the carbonation of air lime.

CHAPTER 2. LIFE CYCLE ASSESSMENT
2.4. LIFE CYCLE INTERPRETATION
In general, this present study performs worse with a net positive GHG result of 0.23 kg CO2 eq.
for the phases A1-A3+B1+C1-C4. The other studies with these phases all performed with a net
negative GHG emission output between -0.44 and -0.56 kg CO2 eq. The two studies not imple-
menting the C-phases have a net negative GHG output of -20.88 and -34.79 kg CO2 eq., which is
significantly higher.
From previous studies above, it is primarily the hemp growth sequestration of CO2 and the car-
bonation of lime that affects the results. The data used in the current study from ecoinvent is not
showing the same output for hemp in the production, here the hemp is not considered carbon zinc.
However, it has not been possible to find the reason why ecoinvent data shows no CO2 uptake.
Also, it should be considered if lime should be calculated as carbon zinc. It releases CO2 when
manufactured and then absorbs it again later in the carbonation process. That would rather be
viewed as a zero-sum game. This accounts for the tree production too, when disposal phases con-
sist of incineration or rotting, the CO2 is released again. Nowadays in the industry, hempcrete is
solely considered a positive product regarding environmental sustainability. This may be related
to outdated data on hemp production, and it is also important to consider the end-of-life phase in
today’s context. This means that previous LCA results did not include a phase that undoubtedly
contributes to a worse outcome of the LCA.
When compared with two traditional insulation, hempcrete performs worse in 11 out of 18 impact
categories for the whole LCA. Hempcrete performs twice as well as stone wool and just slightly
better than aerated concrete in the category of climate change. This is the only parameter counting
today in BR18, thereby hempcrete performs the best when looking at new builds today and the
climate impact demands there are required. All phases are included, thereby even though the hemp
shives and lime are transported 1300 km and stone wool and aerated concrete are local / Danish
produced, the hempcrete performs better.
Figure 2.10: Comparison of impact score Climate change for traditional insulation and alternative
insulation
For the future building industry, the requirements of the products’ environmental performance
could be higher. A more appropriate approach, with more impact categories included, would
result in a more holistic picture. GHG emissions are not the impact affecting the environment.

3
Laboratory experiments
These laboratory experiments have been conducted, as mentioned, to shed light on hypothesis
number 1, which was addressed in section 1.2 and subsection 1.3.1. The laboratory experiments
on hempcrete were conducted to assess the hygrothermal properties of the alternative insulation
material. In total, five experiments were performed to explore this material, along with an infor-
mal CO2 chamber experiment. The CO2 chamber experiment was a rudimentary test aimed at
attempting to accelerate the carbonation process in the test specimens. Overall, the objective was
to gain insights into carbonation, insulation performance, moisture transport and performance,
and general properties such as porosity and density. The following is a list of the experiments
conducted, see figure 3.1, and the subsequent section will provide further elaboration on each of
them and their adjoining results.

Table 3.1: Laboratory experiments conducted on hempcrete
3.1
Test specimens
To cast the hempcrete test specimens, the casting method used was provided by the company
Hemboo. Helena, CEO of Hemboo, helped and instructed the first batch of samples. The process
was overall lime mixed with water and when fully mixed hemp shives were added too. The method
was not fully standardised and thereby some initiative was made in this thesis:
• Mixing time was decided to be 10 minutes. This amount of mixing time was recommended
by Saint Astier making the Batichanvre binder but also the mix seemed homogeneous after
10 minutes using the rule from The Hempcrete Book [51].
• Standardised load was decided to be 2 kg on 100 mm2, corresponding to 0.0196 MPa. In
only one publication a specific load was determined. Here the specimens were tamped at
0.05 MPa [2] and in some other publications it was just recommended to give the hempcrete
a little push by hand, this was also the method used by Hemboo. In this thesis, it was though
38
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.1. TEST SPECIMENS
chosen to have a more standardized way and by inspiration from Cerezo [2] the standardized
load of approx. 0.02 MPa was chosen.
• The test specimens were conditioned in a laboratory air environment for 14 days according
to DS/EN 12390 [4] to ensure that they are dry enough.
Down below in table 3.2 an extract of some of the literature and publications available. It is clear
that both the mixing regime and curing regime differs.

Table 3.2: Mixing and curing regimes of hempcrete by different publications and literature
In general, the procedure of the mixing regime and the curing regime are quite different when
looking at publications or websites. This shows how many possible ways mixing and curing can
take place. There is a lack of a more uniform standardized method for casting hempcrete. In the
hempcrete book [51] the mixing of hempcrete is also described as a process that can easily go
wrong. Here it is stated that there is not much margin for error. The whole book guides those
wanting to cast with hempcrete and a key principle when mixing is correct portions first of all and
to keep the hemp and lime at dry state before mixing. The natural hydraulic lime will start to react
Page 39 of 186
==PAGE:58==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.1. TEST SPECIMENS
when in contact with water and the hemp will absorb water and this could result in rotting while it
is a plant material. The mix-ratio is illustrated, see figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: Mix ratio of hempcrete
Testing of the mix is done by the "Snowball method" described by Helena from Hemboo but also
used in The Hempcrete Book[51]. The Snowball method is simple and straightforward. Firstly
you put some newly mixed hempcrete into your hands and squeeze it into a ball. Afterwards, you
throw it in the air, if it does not fall apart it is good. Then you push a gloved finger into the ball
and if it breaks cleanly, then it is ready for use. Note that if the ball easily crumbles, it indicates
it’s too dry; if your finger sinks into the ball with a "squidgy" feeling, it’s too wet. However, if it
splits in two, then it’s just the right consistency [51]. This method is to some degree questionable
when trying to standardise the product hempcrete because it relies on a subjective to some degree
evaluation of the person mixing hempcrete. In appendix D instructions for casting hempcrete are
given. Down below a list of produced specimens are listed and their adjoining test method, see
table 3.3

3.2. CARBONATION
Figure 3.3: Type 1 test specimens drying after demoulding
3.2
Carbonation
All experiments need to be carried out when all the test specimens are fully complete. This in-
cludes the carbonation of the lime as shown earlier with the lime circle 1.4 in section 1.6 Materials.
The air-lime hardens by being exposed to CO2 and thereby the air lime has to be situated in air
containing CO2. To accommodate optimal conditions, demands on relative humidity, tempera-
ture, and CO2 supply are needed. Hirst [35] found that the carbonation depth is proportional to
the square root of time, meaning the thicker the material the longer the process will take. Thereby
the desired need for a high concentration of CO2 and also as thin specimens as possible. Accord-
ing to another study [31] the critical parameter when enhancing the carbonation process was the
high supply of carbon dioxide but also by optimizing other parameters such as the temperature and
the relative humidity. In the article, it was concluded that hemp-lime would carbonize effectively
by contact with CO2.
Optimal carbonation for air lime occurs when the relative humidity is 50 %, the temperature is
20 ± 2 oC, and a CO2 concentration is 3000 ppm ± 500 ppm. Temperature and relative humidity
were determined on the basis of research studies [35] [31], the Concrete Directory [46], and the
standard DS/EN 12390 [4]. The CO2 concentration in the storage chamber was chosen by follow-
ing the standard DS/EN 12390 [4]. In this standard, the carbonation of concrete is described and
the method is the best suggestion regarding choosing a CO2 concentration in the chamber. When
using a higher concentration of CO2 in the air, the carbonation rate will increase. in general, water
is expelled in the reaction when carbonation occurs. If too much water is present and cannot evap-
orate, the water will fill the pores. It is detected that there is an upper limit for the carbon dioxide
content in the air of 10 % approx. because of the accelerated carbonation [46]. This is why the
test specimens should stay in a conditioned laboratory air environment for 14 days according to
DS/EN 12390 [4] to ensure they are dry enough to start carbonizing in the CO2 chamber.
Page 42 of 186
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.2. CARBONATION
To accelerate the carbonation process a primitive CO2 chamber is built, see figure 3.4, and guid-
ance on building it in Appendix E. The conditions above are complied with by succeeding initia-
tives. In the beginning, a CO2 pump was used, and later, a CO2 bottle, to ensure the supply of
CO2 and raise the CO2 level above normal. The supply of CO2 needed was every 30 minutes
when using the CO2 pump and thereby, the samples were only in the chamber by day. When using
the CO2 bottle, later in the process, the level of CO2 decreased slower. The test specimens are
placed with free space on all sides as seen in figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Closed CO2 chamber with test specimens
It was not possible, but to ensure a temperature of 20 oC, the chamber should have been situated
in a climate room in building 130 at DTU with a constantly monitored temperature of oC. The
safety regarding CO2 leakage was not optimal, hence the chamber was placed in the central lab,
with enough space for leakage. A salt solution of Magnesium Nitrate was used to comply with
the relative humidity demands. According to [32], magnesium nitrate is the closest match with
a relative humidity of 54.38 ± 0.23 % at a temperature of 20 oC. From the standard DS/EN
12390 [4], it is noted from experience that RH, temperature, and CO2 concentration can exceed
the permitted boundaries. Thereby, it is highly recommended and essential to have active control
of these three parameters according to the standard, and this might become a massive problem for
the experiment, which will be looked into later on.

3.2. CARBONATION
The concentration level of CO2 did not differ in 24 hours, meaning that the chamber was sealed
properly. The temperature did vary 1 oC, mainly because the sun went down and building 130,
thereby, might cool down. The RH increased by 10 %, which was a crucial investigation. When
too much water is present, carbonation will not occur. This might suggest that the initiative with
magnesium nitrate and the mini fan did not work as desired.
Figure 3.7: CO2 chamber conditions containing test specimens. Graph is for 1 hour and illustrates
how the CO2 is decreasing and RH is increasing
In figure 3.7, it is clear that the air-lime reacts and the concentration decreases rapidly in one hour.
When the supply of CO2 does not occur, the concentration level is measured at 40 ppm, a factor
10 lower than the atmospheric air. The temperature drops 1 oC, and the RH increases by 6 % RH.
This is expected when water is known to be expelled in the chemical process.
To monitor the carbonation process, a chemical pH indicator is used to indicate the carbonation.
The chemical Thymolphtalein is used, and it has a pH range of 9.9 (9.3-10.5) and changes colour
from colourless, indicating non-alkaline, to blue, indicating alkaline [36]. The indicator is sprayed
on a newly broken surface of a dummy test specimen. The dummies have the same high of 3 cm as
most of the other test specimens and are representative. The indicator illustrates the carbonation
process. The blue colour indicates that the lime is un-carbonized, and colourless indicates full
carbonation when the pH value is below 9.3. This method is easy to use and quasi-quantitative
to determine the carbonation depth in the test specimens. The method is quick and gives reliable
results to the experiment of carbonation. The specimens are sprayed at 5 seconds, ensuring full
coverage of pH indicator of the whole tested area.
Carbonation rate
It is hard to estimate the carbonation rate of the specimens. The particle size of the calcium
dihydroxide will play a role. The bigger, the more available area to react with. The amount of

grams of CO2 that a concentration of 5000 ppm delivers is not at all enough for the carbonation
process to happen for all specimens. This also explains the constant need for CO2 supply. The rate
can be estimated by measuring the carbonated area over time. The penetration can be measured
when looking at the "colourless" distance and dividing it by the associated days.
