3.2. CARBONATION
3.2.2
Discussion
Although the CO2 chamber was controlled and executed as best as one could, the experiment was
insufficient. The tree parameters, temperature, CO2 concentration and relative humidity were im-
possible to control appropriately. This was a significant source of error; the conditions couldn’t be
maintained constant. This is partly because it was challenging to be present and monitor the CO2
chamber manually.
The temperature varied within ±4 degrees, which is considered not decisive. However, the varia-
tion in relative humidity fluctuated significantly, ranging primarily from 40 % to 60 %. At times,
the RH varied significantly, ranging from as low as 25 % to as high as 69 %. This range is higher
than the recommended range of 50 % to 60 %. Therefore, it can be argued that the possibility
of carbonation may have been absent due to the high humidity. Furthermore, a high CO2 con-
centration above the limit of recommended 10,000 ppm was maybe present. It is impossible to
estimate because the equipment is calibrated to 5000 ppm. As a result, there could be a factual
risk of the samples being exposed to elevated CO2 levels for a significant portion of the time. This
would result in "too enhanced carbonation", where the water cannot be released from the samples,
thereby preventing carbonation.
When looking at the carbonation results from the article of Hirst [35], the carbonation depth was
40.3 mm after 21 days without enhanced carbonation of a specimen with the density of 275 kg/m3.
This resulted in an average carbonation rate of 4.7 mm/day [35]. Thereby, it was expected that
some carbonation would have been visible in this thesis. However, if density varied, there was a
clear difference in carbonation, showing a correlation of longer carbonation time with increasing
density. Furthermore, it was discovered that if the density was the same, but the material consisted
of different hemp-lime ratios, the carbonation would too be different [35]. These findings stated
above indicate that the higher density of approx. 390 kg/m3 in this project might cause the reason
for uncarbonated specimens.
The carbonation affects the properties of the test specimen as elaborated in section 1.6 Materi-
als. A carbonated test specimen compared to an uncarbonated test specimen may not result in
significant changes. As it became evident that there would be no fully carbonated test specimens,
Helena, the CEO of Hemboo, was contacted. A few samples, cast approx two years ago, were se-
cured. There was only enough material to create test specimens for two experiments determining
the properties of water vapour permeability and sorption isotherm. Interpretation of the effects of
the uncarbonated specimens is seen throughout the report at each experiment.
For future experiments, conducting them in a chamber with PID, PI, or P control rather than man-
ual control for all parameters is recommended. Additionally, the equipment should be able to
measure CO2 concentration up to 15000 ppm to understand and detect the "too enhanced carbon-
ation" situation. The lack of carbonation in the results is likely due to multiple factors and limited
time. Therefore, it is also recommended to initiate the carbonation process of hempcrete within a
reasonable timeframe, such as within 6 months or more if possible.
Page 48 of 186
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.3. POROSITY & DENSITY
3.3
Porosity & density
The fundamental properties of hempcrete, porosity and density, are determined by two methods.
This is to understand the nature of hempcrete. The density refers to the mass pr. unit volume and
can give insight into the material’s composition, structure, and physical properties. The porosity
describes the voids and volume of space within the material. The properties of the material can
be very much affected by the porosity and give insight into its strength, thermal conductivity,
durability, and ability to absorb or release fluids. The correlation is often that materials with
high porosity have higher thermal conductivity and lower strength. The porosity depends on the
distribution of pores, their size and shape, and if they are open or closed [1]. A simple method is
used to determine the properties, and it is easy and probably best because of the high porosity of
the material. The second method is the LBM2 method, and below, both methods are elaborated.
3.3.1
Simple method
The density and porosity can be estimated quite easily by weighing and measuring the specimens,
and from these numbers have the density. The test specimens are considered dry when the weight
does not change by more than 0.1 % within 24 hours [1]. Water can be bound in porous materials
in both a physical and chemical way. Water molecules can physically adhere to the surfaces of the
pore system when exposed to humid air, reaching a balance with the moisture in the surrounding
environment. Conversely, specific materials, like cement-based materials, can contain chemically
bound water incorporated into the cement gel during mixing. This type of water can only be
eliminated when the gel is dried at temperatures exceeding 105 °C.
Figure 3.10: Measurement for simple method of determining dry porosity and density
The hempcrete is dried to 105 °C to ensure complete dry materials used for calculations. Mea-
surements with a vernier calliper are used to determine the dimensions of the test specimens. The
specimens are measured three times for each diameter, height, length, and width. Here the aver-
age from these measurements is used for the following calculations. Vave is the average volume,
determined by measurements and md is the weight of the dried specimen at 105 oC.
The equation for dry density [3.1]:
ρd = md
Vave

3.3. POROSITY & DENSITY
3.3.2
LBM Test Method 2
The second method used is the LBM2 test method on four test specimens with the dimensions
200x100x50 mm. Firstly the test specimens must be dry with maximum mass changes of 0.1 %
for 4 hours. Afterwards, all specimens are cooled off by using the desiccator containing dried
silica gel. When the room temperature is reached, the specimens are weighted, and the registered
dry mass, md, is recorded. The next phase of the experiment treats the saturated specimens. A
continued evacuation of the specimens for at least three hours under pressure is now conducted,
followed by a supply of boiled and demineralised water at room temperature into the desiccator.
The supply of water stops when all specimens are covered. Here the specimens will be evacuated
for an hour under vapour pressure. After one hour, the pressure will be neutralized and kept in the
desiccator for 24 hours. The saturated test specimens are weighted in water, and the mass msw is
recorded. Afterwards, the surfaces of the specimens are dried with a rag, and the specimens are
again weighted where the mass mssd is recorded. The picture below shows the experimental setup
with the desiccator containing hempcrete and the pump.
Figure 3.11: Experimental setup for LBM method 2
Following parameters are used for calculations of the density and porosity. Room temperature is
20 oC, the demineralised water has a temperature of 20 oC and thereby a density, ρw of 998.16
kg/m3. Formulas from [21] are used for calculations.
Volumen of specimen:

3.3.4
Discussion
For the simple method, the density varied little between Type 3 specimens. However, when the
same method was used for other specimens, types 1, 2, and 4, the dry density did not seem that
consistent. Overall, the average of all types mentioned in the result section gives a dry density of
391 ± 33 kg/m3. It was expected to obtain a density of about 310 kg/m3, and the results show
that even with the attempt to standardize the casting of hempcrete, variation occurs.
When comparing the results from the simple method, they did not align. The average density of
the LBM2 method was 527 kg/m3 with a deviation of 21. This number is maybe too big and the
reason why is the method being unequipped for porous materials. The porosity of the hempcrete
specimen is big and becomes a source of error in this study. When lifting the specimens from the
desiccator to the weight. Some of the water would not stay in the big holes when lifted, and this
would give a lower mass weight of the specimen in the air. This affects the output for the cal-
culated volume of the specimen, being smaller and thereby the dry density becomes higher. The
output of the open porosity will become smaller too, while the volume is smaller. It is assumed
that there may be an uncertainty of up to 5-10 % .
The results displayed a porosity of 73 % in average with a little variation of 0.01. It corresponds
with other data found in literature being in the lower end. The hempcrete examined by other stud-
ies has a dry total porosity of 72 %, 78 % and 79 % [30] for densities at 460, 430 and 430.
Measurement uncertainty occurs when the weight could weigh to an accuracy of ± 0,01 % of the
mass.
Page 52 of 186
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.4. CAPILLARITY
3.4
Capillarity
Capillary suction is a transport phenomenon occurring in porous materials. It occurs because of
the hydrostatic negative pressure in the water just under the meniscus. When a liquid is placed in a
tube or confined space, the surface of the liquid at the interface with the air undergoes a curvature,
forming what is known as a meniscus. Here the surface tension results in an upwards force or the
hydrostatic negative pressure under the meniscus as mentioned before. This force is higher than
the force of gravity and friction of the inside surface of the tube resulting in water rising in the
tube. The suction speed will be reduced with time due to rising inside friction between the water
and the inner wall when the water level rises. Also, the higher the water level the more weight of
water and thereby the higher the force of gravity. By capillarity, the water is transported up through
the material. In a large tube or pore, the meniscus radius increases and thereby, the hydrostatic
pressure decreases, resulting in no capillary suction. The smaller the pores, the bigger the capillary
suction, although if they are too small. To estimate the rate of the capillary suction, the capillary
number, k, is found. This number indicates how fast the suction properties of the material are [1].
In the hempcrete material, the hemp shives act as tubes. In section 1.6, the microscope pictures in
table 1.8, the composition of hemp shives illustrates the tubes inside the material hemp.
3.4.1
The capillary water uptake test, LBM1
The capillary water uptake test LBM1 test specimens have one dimension requirement to fulfil
[22]. The height of the specimens must be at least 30 mm, but the surface area have no certain
specifications. The surface area was chosen considering the carbonation time and by using the
method for surface area specifications for the minimum thickness in the cup method standard.
Thereby the chosen surface area became 0.36 m2 with a thickness of 60 mm for an equilateral
square. The material hempcrete is considered to have a high capillarity and thereby the height
was chosen to be ten times the minimum requirement. In total 3 specimens were tested with the
dimensions 60x60x300 mm. The contact surface must be free from impurities.
When starting the experiment following parameters are recorded; suction surface area, A, the ini-
tial mass, mi, and the height, h. To ensure no washing out occurs, the test specimens are all dried
and have reached a constant mass of 0.1 %. This is obtained by drying the specimens in a venti-
lated oven, maintaining a temp. of 105 ± 5 oC followed by using a desiccator to cool down the
specimens. The constant mass is registered, mo, and compared again by the same procedure after
the experiment. The experiment starts when the specimens are in equilibrium with the laboratory.
The temperature of the room is 21 oC. The temperature of the demineralized water used for the
experiment must be 20 ± 2 oC with an associated density of 998.207 kg/m3.
The specimens are placed on the spacers with the suction surface facing downwards, and water is
poured into the tray, covering approx. 3 mm of the specimens. The timer is started when pouring
in water. When the suction and evaporation occur, the water level will decrease over time, and
thereby, the tray should be refilled to maintain the covering height of approx. 3 mm. Within time
intervals, the specimens are weighed as mt after being wiped with a wring rag. This experiment
stopped after four days, being the threshold at which the experiment should stop according to the
method.
Page 53 of 186
==PAGE:72==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.4. CAPILLARITY
Figure 3.12: Experimental setup to determine the capillary suction

tkap and the corresponding absorbed mass pr. area, Qkap is defined.

this, a time for the error is detected. It is defined terror and is located by the intersection between
the x-axis and the graph. Equation [3.8] calculates the squared Q with compensation for possible
errors. The capillary number, k, and the resistance number, M, are calculated using equations
[3.10] and [3.9].

Discussion
The standard deviation is small at 0.08 for the resistance and 0.0019 for the capillary numbers.
Thereby, there is an agreement between the results for the three samples. The absorbed water to
the breakpoint is approximately 13 kg pr. m2 suction surface area.
In general, no subsidence was recorded for any of the samples. A source of error could be the
highly porous nature of the material, causing water to essentially "drain out" of the test sample

mine the time error for specimen 2.2 since the trend line intersected the y-axis in the first quadrant.

CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.5. WATER VAPOUR PERMEABILITY
The capillary number was twice as high at 0.073 [29] in one study, and another with values rang-
ing from 0.03 to 0.16 [37]. The findings in this present report are in the lower range, being, in the
worst case, a factor 5 too small. This indicates that the high porosity might have been a source of
error influencing the experiment. Is it hard to estimate how much this could affect the results, but
if water fell out, the absorbed mass might have been larger, resulting in a higher absorbed mass
pr. area resulting in a higher capillary number. Also, a source of error might be the uncarbonated
specimen. The porous would have been smaller, leading to an increase in the capillary suction.
When conducting the experiment it was also a possible source of error if the water was absorbed
through the sides. Measurement uncertainty occurs by the weight with an accuracy of ± 0,01 % of
the mass.
The experimental results of capillary suction and resistance number are utilized in further com-
parative analysis in chapter 5, where the property is compared with traditional insulation materials
to address hypothesis 1. The results are also incorporated into hygrothermal calculations and
simulations in chapter 4 to address and answer hypothesis 1.
3.5
Water vapour permeability
Water vapour permeability refers to the ability of a material to allow water vapour to pass through
it. The parameter is vital in the moisture transportation of the material and happens when an un-
even vapour pressure on either side of the material occurs. To reach equilibrium, the water vapour
will try to penetrate the material, and some materials have a higher or lower resistance against it.
Several water vapour permeability coefficients are investigated. The water vapour permeance,
W, measures how quickly water vapour passes through a material in relation to the partial vapour
pressure. The water vapour resistance, Z characterizes the material’s ability to resist moisture
transport. The water vapour permeability, δ, reflects the material’s capacity to allow water vapour
penetration. Lastly, a parameter is the water vapour resistance factor, µ, which quantifies the ma-
terial’s resistance to vapour penetration and flow compared to an equivalent air layer, which can
be determined.
3.5.1
Cup method
The cup method is frequently used to document the hygroscopic behaviour of materials because
it is a documented measurement method available in the hygroscopic range. The cup method fol-
lows the guidelines outlined in DS/EN ISO 12572 [7]. Multiple criteria concerning the specimens
used are adhered to in this approach. The specimens should have a circular shape with a diameter
at least twice the height of the specimens. The specimens are approx. 30 mm and the diameter is
80 mm, complying with the demands. The exposed area should be at least 0.005 m2, allowing a
minimum diameter of 79.8 mm. A few test specimens are on the verge of not meeting the standard
requirements, see table C.
The height of the test specimens was not complying with the demand that an inhomogeneous
material such as hempcrete must have a height minimum of three times the largest particle size.
Page 56 of 186
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.5. WATER VAPOUR PERMEABILITY
The average hemp shive is 16 mm, which is not even the largest high likely, and thereby the min-
imum thickness should be 48 mm. This is a source of error for later discussion. The height of
30 mm was selected because it was the maximum feasible height achievable with the cup method
equipment. Nine test specimens were tested; three experiments were conducted with the wet cup
method and six with the dry cup method. It was possible to obtain three carbonated specimens
from a two-year-old batch from Hemboo, and thereby the specimens for this experiment is with
carbonated and uncarbonated hempcrete. Table 3.7 below show the test specimens tested. All test
specimens are measured four times, and the average dimensions are used for further calculations.

Figure 3.14: Testing of carbonation by the indicator Thymolphthalein
The picture in figure 3.14 indicates that the specimens are almost entirely carbonated.
To conduct the cup test, the specimens are prepared by sealing the sides of each specimen with
plastic rings measuring ø100. Epoxy is used as the sealing material and will be placed between
the specimen edge and the ø100 ring. This sealing aims to prevent vapour diffusion through the
sides of the specimen, effectively restricting vapour diffusion to the designated surface area. By
doing this, the masked edge correction is overlooked for further calculations; see Annex F in the
standard ISO 12572 [7]. The whole procedure of prepping the specimens is elaborated detailed in
Appendix I. Picture of a finished and ready test specimen is illustrated, see figure 3.15.
Page 57 of 186
==PAGE:76==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.5. WATER VAPOUR PERMEABILITY
Figure 3.15: Cup method test specimen 1.2 in plastic ring sealed with epoxy
Before the cup experiment is initiated, the test specimens are conditioned in a controlled environ-
ment until a constant weight is obtained. The steady environment consists of a temperature of 23
± 5 oC and a relative humidity of 50 ± 5. This is done by placing the specimens in a laboratory
chamber with these conditions and the specimens are conditioned when three successive daily
readings of the weight will differ no more than 5 %. A minimum of 6 hours of storage time is
required. When the weight is stabilized the experiment can start.
The desiccant delivers an RH of 0 % at a temp. of 23 ± 5 oC for the dry cup experiment is
silicagel [7]. The potassium nitrate, KNO3, is used as the saturated salt solution delivering an
RH of 94 % for the wet cup experiment [7]. The silica gel or KNO3 is placed in the cups with
a minimum depth of 15 mm to ensure the wanted climate in the cup. Afterwards, the cups are
sealed with rubber rings and a "screw mechanism", see figure 3.16. To ensure the calculations of
the resistance, later on, the measuring of the air gap between the desiccant or saturated solution
and the specimen must be noted. This air gap should be 15 ± 5 mm. In the wet cup test, moisture
transport will be out of the cup, and reversely in the dry cup test, and moisture transport will be
into the cup. A projection of the test cup is shown below in figure 3.16
Page 58 of 186
==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.5. WATER VAPOUR PERMEABILITY
Figure 3.16: Projection of the test cup used for the Cup Method
It is crucial not to tilt the cup and have the potassium nitrate touch the test specimen. Then the test
is ruined. The cups are placed in the test chamber and weighted in time intervals selected daily.
The weighting continued for 14 days and stopped when five successive weightings were constant
within ± 5 % as the standard requires. All formulas used are given by DS ISO 12572 [7].

3.5.3
Discussion
Data is acceptable and considered stable when having a correlation coefficient generally very close
to 1, see appendix I. The coefficient R2 was between 0.9933 and 0.9983 except for one coefficient
for Cup 8 with a correlation of 0.9702, which is still very fine. The results show a permeable
material, both in the wet cup and dry cup experiments. Note that the wet cup experiment showed
more deviation in the results compared to the dry cup experiments. By wet cup experiments, the
deviation could vary between 8 % to 23 % compared to a deviation of 3 % to 10 % for the dry
cup for the Water vapour permeability and the water vapour permeance. For the water vapour
resistance factor and the water vapour flow rate the contrast between deviation for wet and dry
was even higher hence 8 % to 41 % compared to 3 % to 8 %. In general, something must have
been off for the test Cup 1 and 3. Here the deviation is too big from all other data.
Results are consistent with the datasheet of Batichanvre stating that the permeability would be 1.5
10−11kg · m−1 · s−1 · Pa−1. Water vapour permeability, δ, results are consistent with already
existing data on hempcrete from literature where one study [30] finds hempcrete 1.7 × 10−11 to
6.2 × 10−11kg · m−1 · s−1 · Pa−1 and another study [18] to 3.74 × 10−11kg · m−1 · s−1 · Pa−1.
Water vapour resistance, Z, varies below 3 GPa · s · m2/kg, which indicates a vapour-permeable
material. The hempcrete cannot act as a vapour diffusion retarder, especially not as a vapour
barrier, according to SBI 207 [28]. Water vapour resistance factor, µ does too correspond with
existing experiments on hempcrete. A study [18] has a resistance factor of 5.35, being in between
the factor of this study varying from 4.71 to 12.50.
Page 62 of 186
==PAGE:81==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.6. SORPTION ISOTHERMS
When investigating the differences between the carbonized and uncarbonized specimens, tenden-
cies are clear. The material’s permeability becomes greater as hempcrete approaches full carbon-
ation for the wet and dry cup experiment, supported by the Z-value decrease. Hempcrete becomes
less resistant to moisture transport during full carbonation. The δ-values and W-values are higher
for the carbonized test specimens, and the reverse is shown for the Z-values and µ-values. This
tendency is consistent with the known theory about the carbonation process of lime and the appur-
tenant changing properties.
The carbonated sample should follow the same specifications as the samples produced in this
experiment. Different mix-ratio, approach, or third factors may have influenced the carbonated
hempcrete, potentially causing it to have other changed parameters and not only carbonation as
the only variation. This is a source of error that cannot be altered but can only be speculated
upon. However, the numbers are generally not far apart. Measurement uncertainty occurs when
the weight was capable of weighing to an accuracy of ± 0,01 % of the mass.
The experimental results of permeability and resistance factor are utilized in further compara-
tive analysis in chapter 5, where the property is compared with traditional insulation materials to
address hypothesis 1. The results are also incorporated into hygrothermal calculations and simu-
lations in chapter 4 to address and answer hypothesis 1.
3.6
Sorption isotherms
Porous materials possess hygroscopic properties, allowing them to absorb moisture from the sur-
rounding air. The amount of water absorbed increases with higher relative humidity. This process
is known as adsorption. Conversely, when the material releases moisture, it is referred to as des-
orption. A sorption isotherm curve represents the relationship between RH and water content at
a fixed temperature. The hygroscopic sorption is water exchange and is present until the equilib-
rium point is reached between the water and the ambient air. In the climate chamber method, the
sorption isotherm curve is determined for the hempcrete material. The main component making
the hempcrete hygroscopic is the hemp shives since they have a large internal surface where water
is sorbed.
3.6.1
Climate chamber method
The climate chamber method follows DS/EN ISO 12571 [6]. In general, the chamber should be
capable of maintaining the relative humidity within ± 5 % RH and the temperature within ± 2 K
over the whole test area. This is done by having two hoes supplying dry and moist air and a fan
distributing it evenly in the chamber. The whole chamber is controlled by PID control, and below
in figure 3.20, the test setup of the climate chamber containing all test specimens is shown.
Page 63 of 186
==PAGE:82==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.6. SORPTION ISOTHERMS
Figure 3.20: Climate chamber method test setup
It is required that a minimum of three test specimens are tested by the climate chamber method. In
this experiment, three specimens of the carbonated material are tested, and three specimens of the
not fully carbonated material are tested. It is also required that a specimen should be representa-
tive of the product and that the density of the material would determine the form. If the density is
higher than 300 kg/m3 the test specimens should consist of at least 10 g. of the material. This is
the case for the carbonated specimens resulting in the size of dimensions 50x50x30 mm, weighing
more than 10 grams. The specimens cast in the thesis were expected to have a density of 310
kg/m3, and the standard requires that all test specimens with a density lower than 300 kg/m3
should have an area of at least 100x100 mm. To take into account that the density might be just
below the threshold value, it was chosen to cast the hempcrete in moulds having a dimension of
100x100x30 mm. Later, when looking into the equipment of the climate chamber, it was discov-
ered that the only dimension fitting into the petri dish was 50x50 mm in area. This resulted in the
samples being cut up to fit the petri dish.
A total of 3 test pieces of size 100x100 mm were split and 3 pieces from each of these samples
were included in the experiment. This gave a total of 12 samples, respectively 9 uncarbonated and
3 carbonated samples. The choice to test all 9 samples and not just 3 was due to the fact, that the
density varied from batch to batch, and thus it could be interesting to see if this means anything
for the hygroscopic properties.

Adsorption
The concept of the climate chamber method for determining the adsorption curve is to increase
the relative humidity in a test environment with a constant temperature of 20 ± 5 oC. The test
specimen is in the beginning, dried to constant mass and placed in the chamber. Afterwards, the
RH is increased to different fixed RHs and once each environment is in equilibrium, the moisture
content is determined. The weighing of the sample is done until a constant mass is obtained when
there is established equilibrium with the surroundings.
To determine the constant dried mass at the beginning of the experiment the specimens are dried in
a ventilated oven at a temp. of 105 ± 5 oC. If the difference in mass between three measurements,
each taken at least 24 hours apart, is less than 0.1 % of the total mass, the constant mass has been
obtained. The drying procedure complies with and follows the standard EN ISO 12750.
The weighting of the sample at equilibrium is repeated for increasing humidities and a minimum
of four humidities approx. evenly spaced in the range of 30 to 95 should be chosen according
to the standard. The procedure for determining the constant mass at different RHs is the same as
when determining the constant mass after drying the test specimen. It will thereby take 3 days each
time a specimen’s constant mass is determined. It would be preferable to have a lot of intervals of
RHs to get a more accurate absorption curve, but with respect to the time, it was chosen to have
only seven different RHs in the experiment:
30, 50, 70, 80, 85, 92 and 94 % RF
Desorption
After adsorption curves are determined for the test specimen, the following experiment should
be desorption. Here it is investigated how the hempcrete will release the moisture and thereby
the starting point for desorption is at least 95 % RH according to [6]. The procedure for the
absorption experiment is almost alike except for the adjustment, that the RH now is decreasing.
The specimens are periodically weighted in the climatic chamber until it is in equilibrium with the
Page 65 of 186
==PAGE:84==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.6. SORPTION ISOTHERMS
environment. This equilibrium is reached when constant mass is reached, which is if the change
of mass between three consecutive weighings, each made 24 h apart, is less than 0,1 % of the total
mass. A minimum of four relative humidities between 95 % and 30 % relative humidity, roughly
evenly spaced, must be chosen. In consideration of the same time aspects as before, it is chosen to
fewer RH’s than desired. Again six different intervals were chosen for the desorption experiment:
94, 90, 85, 75, 50 and 30 % RF
In the sorption isotherm, the moisture content is plotted against the relative humidity. Based on
the estimated mass at moisture equilibrium, m, and the dry mass, m0, for a particular relative
humidity, the equilibrium water content in mass%, u, is calculated by equation [3.15].
u = m −m0
m0

[%]
(3.15)
Generally, it was desired to have even higher relative humidities up to 96 % or more. However,
the equipment used was unable to achieve such high levels, and as a result, the relative humidity
never reached as high as hoped for in both curves. In total, it took two months to conduct the
experiment.

gained an average of 5 mass%.

3.6.3
Discussion
The sorption isotherms in the illustration demonstrate that all specimens exhibit fairly similar be-
haviour with very little variance. Higher RH’s generally result in an increase in the deviation. The
adsorption and desorption curves are nearly parallel for most humidities, and in the range above
80 % RH the capillary condensation starts to occur. It is observed that hysteresis is present for
the sorption isotherm curve, leaving the hempcrete after desorption with an increase in moisture
content compared to the beginning of the adsorption curve, respectively approx. 5 mass% higher
for uncarbonated specimens and 2 mass% higher for carbonated specimens. This can indicate that
the material has a certain form of moisture storage or retention, which might be a possible problem
for a building component of hempcrete.
For all carbonated samples, a mass percentage of 9.3 to 10 is observed at 94 % RH. In contrast,
the non-carbonated samples have a mass percentage lower at 8.4 mass% based on drying at 105
°C. The carbonated specimens could bind more water molecules to the inner surfaces than the un-
carbonated ones. In the desorption curve, the water release was higher for the carbonated sample
than the uncarbonated one, indicating that carbonated hempcrete has an easier time absorbing and
releasing moisture. Observing this tendency is expected, while the theory of carbonation indicates
higher permeability of lime when carbonated. Again, as mentioned earlier, a source of error might
occur when not being certain of the carbonated hempcrete samples’ mixing, casting and origin. It
is claimed the same material and process. The ability to absorb and desorb water from the air was
expected to be the best of a carbonated specimen.
The curve reflects the sorption behaviour of hempcrete in a range of 30 % to 94 % of RH. It would
be interesting to have more detailed data, including a wider range of RH values, both in the low
and high spectrum. It is not expected to differ from the already seen directions of the curves, but
to be even more precise is preferable. Also, the demand of reaching at least 95 % RH was not
Page 68 of 186
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.7. THERMAL CONDUCTIVITY
obtained because of the equipment being unable to reach it.
The results generally comply with a research study [30]. For moulded hempcrete based on drying
at 105 °C the weight percentage was 11.5 at 94 RH and at 30 RH the weight percentage was 4.
This is almost the same ± 1 mass% for this present study with a corresponding uniform course of
the isotherm curve too. Measurement uncertainty occurred where the weight had an accuracy of
± 0,01 % of the mass. Furthermore, the chamber had an uncertainty of measuring and controlling
the RH at ± 0,01 %.
The experimental results of sorption isotherms are utilized in further comparative analysis in chap-
ter 5, where the property is compared with traditional insulation materials to address hypothesis
1. The results are also incorporated into hygrothermal calculations and simulations in chapter 4 to
address and answer hypothesis 1.
3.7
Thermal conductivity
Heat transfer is divided into three main types; convection, radiation and conduction. Thermal
conductivity primarily describes the material’s ability to transfer heat and is typically measured in
watts per meter-kelvin (W/mK). It describes the rate at which heat energy is transferred through
a material when there is a temperature difference between two points on opposite ends of the ma-
terial. It is preferable to have a low thermal conductivity when looking at an insulation material
because a material with low thermal conductivity will transfer heat more slowly.
Various factors affect the thermal conductivity of a material, such as air content, moisture ratio,
temperature, and air tightness. All components have a thermal conductivity and some are lower
than others. Stagnant air has a thermal conductivity of 0.025 W/(mK), organic materials have
0.3-0.4 W/(mK), and inorganic materials have around 1-12 W/(mK). As a poor heat conductor,
the air trapped in the pores of a porous material lowers the material’s overall thermal conductivity.
Thereby, if the material’s porosity is big, the material will perform better in regards to thermal
conductivity. The link between thermal conductivity and porosity, however, can be intricate and
change based on the particular properties of the material, such as pore size, pore shape, and the
kind of gas or fluid filling the holes [1].
The moisture ratio in a material affects the thermal conductivity, while the thermal conductiv-
ity of water is 0.6 W/(mK) and thereby approx. 23 times higher than stagnant air. Also, it is
relatively high when compared to many other materials too. An even small amount of moisture
can significantly reduce the material’s thermal conductivity [1]. Also, the density of the material
correlates with the thermal conductivity. When the density is high, the particles in the material are
more closely packed, allowing for more effective heat transfer through particle collisions. In the
article by Cerezo [2], a relationship between thermal conductivity (K) and density (ρ) was found
for hempcrete by following equation 3.16.
K = 0.0002 ∗ρ + 0.0194
[

m ∗K ]
(3.16)
This will be calculated for the product to see if the hempcrete in this thesis behaves as Cerezo
Page 69 of 186
==PAGE:88==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.7. THERMAL CONDUCTIVITY
would suggest from the formula. The experimental way to determine the thermal conductivity of
the hempcrete specimen is done by the guarded hot plate (GHP) method. It is a complex setup,
and the experiment will be elaborated in the next section.
3.7.1
Guarded hot plate
The GHP experiment follows DS/EN 12664 [5]. The design of the experiment is overall a sand-
wich concept with the test specimen in the middle of a cooling and heating unit. The temperature
difference between the test specimen’s surfaces, the reference temperature at the specimen’s cold
side, and the hot plate’s effect are all considered to apply Fourier’s law.
All test specimens are of the dimensions 300x300x30 mm, and they are tested in a dried-out state.
The area of the specimens is made to comply with the heating apparatus surface. The thickness is
chosen to be 30 mm considering the carbonation time as explained in section 3.2. The thickness
complies too with the maximum and minimum allowed specimen thickness described in the stan-
dard [5]. The dry state condition of the hempcrete specimen is achieved by drying the material in
a ventilated oven at a temp. of 105 ± 5 oC. When equilibrium is reached, the experiment can be
conducted. The equilibrium is reached when ∆w < ∆w1. The ∆w defines the change in moisture
content over time and the ∆w1 defines the appropriate limiting value of moisture content change,
Annex F in DS/EN 12664 [5].
No edge insulation is used in this experiment, but it is required if the experiment was operating
above or below room temperature. For rigid materials such as hempcrete, the faces shall be as flat
as the apparatus surfaces and be parallel within 2 % over the whole surface. The test specimens
were 3.5 %. The flatness of the hempcrete did not fully comply, and thereby, thin sheets of foam
that can be compressed are inserted between the surfaces and the plates of the apparatus. This
ensures good thermal contact between the apparatus and the test specimen.
The plate apparatus is 300x300 mm, but the actual measuring area is 160x160 mm, centred in
the middle of the plate apparatus. This ensures that the one-dimensional heat flow is the only
thing measured and prevents temperature gradients in other directions. When the thin foam con-
tact sheets are used, the thermocouples are mounted in above mentioned measuring area on the
surface of the specimen. Five thermocouples are placed on each side of the test specimen, and
a temperature sensor is placed on the upper side of the specimen. A Benchlink logger is linked
to the cables that measure the voltage over the thermocouples, and the voltage is then expressed
as a percentage of a predetermined reference value. A standard voltmeter measures the voltage
over the heating plate, which remains constant throughout the experiment. This voltmeter is too
connected to the Benchlink logger, and all data is plotted in the Benchlink logger program on a
PC, see Appendix J for setup.
The cooling unit has to be precooled by demineralized water and connected to a Heto. When
the valve is opened, it takes many hours to get the cooling water to a temperature of 12.5 oC. This
is to avoid possible condensation. Many problems were faced using the cooling bath, and it took
two days to reach a reasonable temperature at 11.6 oC, which became the temp of the cooling bath
Page 70 of 186
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.7. THERMAL CONDUCTIVITY
for this thesis’s GHP experiment. To prepare for testing, each specimen’s mass, m, and thickness,
d, are determined prior to placement in the apparatus.
Figure 3.23: GHP experiment setup in building 118
The material’s thermal conductivity is calculated by equation 3.17. This value must be reported
at the test’s mean temperature because it is temperature-dependent. The typical temperature for
measurements in the plate equipment at DTU will frequently be about 24.5°C. A represents the
measuring area, d represents the thickness of the test specimen, ∆T, represents the temperature
diff. over the test specimen, and Φ is the power of the heating plate.
λ =

A ∗∆T

in °C, see equation 3.18. u is the voltage over the thermocouples, and n is the number of thermo-
couples.
∆T = u

3.7.3
Discussion
The thermal conductivity given by Hemboo was 0.08 W/mK, which complies with the results from
the GHP experiment shown below in table 3.13. The deviation at 0.0018 is considered small equal
to an agreement between the results of the GHP experiment. The difference was though 16 to 25
% comparing Cerezo and obtained results. This implies that the density cannot stand alone when
determining thermal conductivity. However, it can provide a close estimate. The assumption that
density and heat conductivity follow one another does not make as much sense. The lowest ther-
mal conductivity of the three is seen in the specimens with medium density. A study by Pretot [26]
Page 72 of 186
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 3. LABORATORY EXPERIMENTS
3.7. THERMAL CONDUCTIVITY
showed that mostly density did affect the thermal performance of hempcrete. It is noted that the
binder type, the hemp shiv type, and the hemp-to-binder ratio cause the thermal conductivity value
to diverge from the variation law in addition to the classic rise in thermal conductivity with density.
To evaluate the results, other articles are included as reference values. The proportion of hemp
and the type of binders employed in the mixture vary widely from author to author. Thereby it is
hard to find literature directly equal to the results of this thesis.
Study
2013 [26]
2014 [23]
2019 [48]
Density
250 to 600
377 to 603
250 to 500
Thermal conductivity
0.085 to 0.179
0.0899 and 0.1408
0.3 and 0.9 W/(m2K)
Table 3.14
In the study from 2014 [23] it was concluded that the ratio of hemp-binder did have a significant
effect. The more hemp shives, the better thermal performance was seen. It also corresponds with
the fact that the binder has the highest density and that hemp shives can create air holes and make
the material porous, thereby making it an insulation material. In this thesis, 1/3 is hemp shives, and
2/3 is lime binder. When trying to find this ratio in the above-mentioned literature, the λ values
obtained in this GHP experiment seem reasonable and reliable.
A source of error in this study might be that the specimens were placed in the GHP equipment in
a dry state. As mentioned, water influences the thermal performance of a material, and the RH in
the room was at 50 %. The material’s moisture transport might cause errors when experimenting,
while absorption from moisture in the air occurs. When comparing water content with density
regarding thermal performance, density significantly influences thermal conductivity more than
moisture content. A research article [26] demonstrated that the heat conductivity improves by less
than 20 % from a dry condition to 90 % RH, but by roughly 54 % when the density increases
by 2/3. It might be neglected that the RH could have significantly affected this experiment when
the differences in RH were not that big. Another source of error causing uncertainty could be
the uneven and rigid surface of the test specimen, when the contact isn’t complete between the
specimen and the hot and cold plates, regardless of the foam layer in between. With thermo-
couples, generally, the bigger the temperature differential between the measurement points and/or
the environment, the greater the measurement uncertainty resulting from heat conduction in the
measuring leads to/from the environment and/or the measurement points. Thereby uncertainty of
measurement is present for the thermocouples measuring the difference over the test specimen. It
is assessed to be approx. ± 0,2 K, and when the temperature diff. is measured to approx. 7.5 K,
resulting in an error on the temperature measurement of (0.2/7.5*100) 2.6 %. The measurement
of the voltage might, too, have errors. The voltmeter is considered to have an uncertainty of ap-
prox. 1 %, and this measurement affects the thermal conductivity. The error of measurement of
the thermal conductivity is 2.6 % + 1 %, being 3.6 % in total.
The experimental results of thermal conductivity are utilized in further comparative analysis in
chapter 5, where the property is compared with traditional insulation materials to address hypoth-
esis 1. The results are also incorporated into hygrothermal calculations and simulations in chapter
4 to address and answer hypothesis 1.
Page 73 of 186
==PAGE:92==
4
Hygrothermal calculations
The hygrothermal calculations has been conducted, as mentioned, to analyse and answer hypoth-
esis number 1, which was addressed in section 1.2 and subsection 1.3.1. The thermal calculations
are done in section 4.1, the hydrothermal calculation is done in section 4.2.1 and simulations by
Delphin 6.1 of the hydrothermal performance are done in section 4.3. A typical wall of hempcrete
construction type is investigated regarding insulation and moisture performance and done by uni-
dimensional calculations and dynamic simulations. The wall is based on information from Hem-
boo and the book ’The Hempcrete Book’ [51]. Below is figure 4.2 illustrating the projection of
the wall constructions. Generally, the rule of thumb is that the thickness of hempcrete insulation
should range between 300 to 400 mm, and the coverage of hempcrete over the structural timber
should be greater than or equal to the thickness of the timber [51]. Also, the facing should always
be permeable.
To ensure the hygrothermal performance of the hempcrete wall, it is crucial to apply hygrothermal
renders. In principle, the rule of thumb is that any plaster can be used when it is ensured that it
is at least as vapour permeable as the hempcrete behind it [51]. Also, the render, in collaboration
with the hempcrete, ensures good airtightness [51]. For this thesis, a lime plaster NHL 2.0 is used
by the company Saint Astier as exterior render.
Figure 4.1: Projection of wall construction used in hygrothermal calculations

in the construction calculates the U-value. The transition thermal resistance is set according to DS
418; 0.04 (m2 ∗K)/W on the exterior side and 0.13 (m2 ∗K)/W for interior surfaces.

Discussion
The calculated U-value in table 4.1 is 0.189 W/(m2 ∗K) and thereby fulfils the requirement of
0.30 W/(m2 ∗K). To have a even better U-value the thickness needs to be extended or additional
insulation material in cohesion with hempcrete could be applied. Thermal bridges are minimal
when using buildings in situ with hempcrete due to complete insulation around all joints. If the
hempcrete is not applied to all parts of the building, the existence of possible thermal bridges
would be interesting to determine.
4.2
Moisture calculations
Temperatures between 0 and 50 °C are ideal for mould growth. This sufficient moisture is a key
factor in how mould grows. It is considered that RH values above 75 % foster the best circum-
stances for the growth of mould fungus [1].
There are possible ways that moisture can occur in buildings. The moisture sources can be external
and internal. External sources are often present if the building envelope is exposed to driving rain.
Here there is a risk of build-up moisture from the outside and in. Other moisture sources are con-
densation, rising damp, or inner moisture in the envelope. All building components should avoid
these moisture sources, and in the following, it is investigated by simulations and calculations if a
hempcrete wall avoids those.
To understand the limit values for wood, the illustration below from BYG-ERFA is used, see figure
4.3. On the x-axis is the moisture content of wood and on the y-axis is the temperature. Growth
starts at temperatures above 6 oC, and the yellow area displays the normal limit for fungus attack.
This results in a maximum allowed moisture content of 20 % for wood when trying to avoid
degradation.
Page 76 of 186
==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER 4. HYGROTHERMAL CALCULATIONS
4.2. MOISTURE CALCULATIONS
Figure 4.3: Degradation of wood in buildings. Source: BYGEFRA [20]. X-axis is the moisture
content of the wood, y-axis is temperature, orange area: fungus attack at special conditions, yellow
area: normal limit for fungus attack and the grey area: the limit for optimal conditions.
As stated in the introduction, the requirements from BR18 [53] have no specific limits. Overall
damage to the building or health risks by moisture is not allowed. Several initiatives are done
to determine if a wall constructed in hempcrete fulfils these requirements. Firstly a stationary
calculation by the Glaser method using the standard DS/EN 13788 [10] is conducted to evaluate
the moisture, and secondly, simulations in the program Delphin 6.1 is conducted. The simulation
is conducted to overcome the limits existing in the simplified calculation method of Glaser.
4.2.1
Glaser method
The Glaser method gives a rough estimate of the moisture conditions. The water vapour diffu-
sion is calculated, and constant conditions on both sides of the construction are assumed in this
method. The calculations follow DS/EN ISO 13788 [38] and are based on stationary conditions.
An overview of moisture ingress and drying as a function of time can be obtained by calculating
in monthly intervals. Using the Glaser method, a simple temperature and vapour pressure profile
of the cross-section of the wall is calculated.
Boundary conditions: The outdoor temperature is determined from the Design Reference Year
(DRY) for the monthly average value, θe. The weather data file is DRY 2001-2010 from the
stations "Holbæk, DMI og Sjælsmark". The indoor temperature is assumed to be constant at
20 °C. The internal humidity is calculated by Interior Humidity Class 2, considering occupied
building. Here the moisture addition should be 4 g/m3 relative to the outdoor air for the winter
months, decreasing toward warmer months. Appendix A, figure A.2 are used to calculate indoor
RH.

One calculation was done for the worst case for the year: (DRY) data followed -15 °C with 100
% RH. The calculations resulted in a condensation point between external plaster and hempcrete
with a 150 % RH. This gave a condensation of 0.137 kg/m2 pr. month when having adjusted the
vapour pressure profile. The wall is wet at worst-case conditions.
Discussion
No interstitial condensation occurs within the structure in any month using mean temperature val-
ues. As a result, condensation shouldn’t be a problem in the structure and thereby bu usable in
construction. However, the RH undesirable high at 94.4 to 99.6 % in the period November to
March, almost reaching 100 % RH. Even if the pH value should keep the fungus and mould away,
it is not certain that it can provide sufficient protection with high RHs for 5 months.
The structural element of the hempcrete wall consists of wood. This is a moisture-sensitive ma-
terial, and when the temperature is between 20 and 30 °C and the RH is between 80 % and 90
%, mould in wood develops most effectively. The temperature is not high enough in the months
when the high humidity occurs. However, having the wooden structure exposed to high RH for
an extended period is not optimal. The wood chosen for the construction should not exceed 20
mass%; this is the boundary for no risk of attack from fungus. Looking at the isotherm curves of
different woods, the mass% at the RH range of 80 % to 95 % are, respectively, plywood; 18 to
27, pinewood; 18 to 28, chipboard; 19 to 29 [1]. However, fungi require nutrients, oxygen, mois-
ture, heat and light according to BYG-EFRA [20]. Almost all parameters are absent when having
hempcrete cast around the structure, but some risk might be present in the wood. When calculating
a worst-case scenario, it showed a critical condensation situation in the wall. Calculations with
dynamic parameters such as temperatures throughout the year are necessary.

4.3. SIMULATION PROGRAM DELPHIN 6.1
4.3
Simulation program Delphin 6.1
Delphin 6.1 utilises fixed and moisture-dependent material parameters as a calculation basis, and
climate data as driving rain and radiation is incorporated in the calculation, making the climate
loads more realistic. Down below are facts about the program used for this report.
Software
Dimension
Updated
Moisture transport
Delphin 6.1
1, 2

Table 4.5: Information about simulation software Delphin 6.1
All input parameters in Delphin 6.1 and documentation of these adhere to the guidelines provided
by SBI 277 [28]. Climate data, material properties, initial conditions for the calculations, resis-
tances transition and grid point are presented in Appendix L, and some are elaborated briefly in
the following.
4.3.1
Method
Orientation for the simulations is both South, East, West and North for the hempcrete wall initially.
Further simulations will be for the west because it displays the most critical simulation, while the
driving rain in Denmark primarily comes from the southwest. Input parameters are obtained from
the experiments for the hempcrete material. The simulation period is set to five years, while it is
expected that moisture might occur in the wall and a long period is good to observe, to see have
the hygrothermal properties of the wall are over years.
Figure 4.4: Construction of hempcrete wall in Delhpin 6.1
The exterior plaster is an NHL 2.0 chosen from the provider Saint Astier. Most properties are
collected in a research article doing experiments on NHL 2.0 [14]. The interior plaster is chosen
from a provider CalceCanapa [24], and the company provide adequate values. These materials are
made in the program by choosing an already existing material and modifying it.

A material property that shows a factor of 5 difference compared to findings in other studies is the
capillary number for hempcrete. Therefore, this parameter is being investigated, including three
cases for the k-value: 0.033 (present study), 0.073 [29], and 0.16 [37].
To evaluate the simulation results the criteria below are clarified.
• The moisture content of the wall is stabilized to ensure that the wall doesn’t soak up water
over time.
• A mass percentage of a maximum of 20 % is permissible for construction wood of class 0*.
Generally, it should not exceed 20 Mass% in the wall in all layers to prevent deterioration
in organic materials.
• No mould growth on the internal surface of the wall by complying with the limit of the
isopleth graph by Sedlbauer [50].
• No mould growth on outer surface by using the VTT mould index, this can be investigated.
The index goes from 0 no growth to 6 being 100 % coverage. The mould index describing
all categories are shown in Appendix [15].
• Determination of how driving rain affects the wall performance
• Presence of relative humidity at 100 % and above should not occur, while it indicates the
dew water and that free saturation is present. In permeable materials it is not possible
Page 81 of 186
==PAGE:100==
CHAPTER 4. HYGROTHERMAL CALCULATIONS
4.3. SIMULATION PROGRAM DELPHIN 6.1
to indicate the condensation water because the transition from sorption moisture and dew
water happens gradually.
• The construction should not lose insulation performance by becoming wet
The evaluation criteria are from Delphin website, tutorial: assessment [15]. *This class is typically
associated with wood used in interior construction, such as furniture, flooring, or paneling.

looking at overall simulations L for North, South, East and West, the worst case is West. The
wall’s temperature, relative humidity and water content profiles are illustrated in graphs by the
post-processer program.

The figure of RH comparison by colour map, see 4.11, shows the significant accumulation of
moisture throughout time performing worse with increasing capillary number.
4.3.3
Discussion
Moisture storage is changing by rising in the winter and decreasing in the summer with respec-
tively approx. 13-17 kg/m2 and 7-9 kg/m2. The water content increases in the first year and is
high every cold period because the conditions make it hard to remove moisture. The water con-
tent seems almost stationary for the south wall over the years. However, the water content in the
simulation for the West seems to increase slightly by approx. 2 kg/m2 pr. year, see figure 4.6.
When the curve of RH is high in the outer wall and decreases inwards, it is assumed to be external
moisture rain that causes the moisture increase. If the curve would start lower in the external layer
and slowly increase through the construction, followed by a decrease toward the inner layer, it
could have been a sign of condensation.
The relative humidity profile displayed in figure 4.7 by the colour chart illustrates that the RH is
above 80 % in the first half of the wall. The construction wood is located in the middle of the wall,
which could indicate a risk of deterioration due to the mass% of moisture in the wood reaching
the limit of 20 mass%, see section 4.2. The moisture content for hempcrete is only reaching 12
mass% by simulation, see appendix L. The criteria for a wall with construction below 20 mass-%
are complied with, but the simulation is 1D, missing the structural wood. There is a risk of deteri-
oration of the wood. Thereby, changes to the exterior render could be applied to accommodate the
criteria because it is soaking too much water, thereby the gateway to the high humidities.
The water content in the exterior render is examined. It is generally very high, between 200 and
500 kg/m3, because of the exposition to rain, see appendix L. In winter, it is worse because of
the lack of high temperatures and low humidity to help dry the render. Generally, external render
absorbs a significant amount of water throughout the year, which is not optimal. The outer surface
Page 86 of 186
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 4. HYGROTHERMAL CALCULATIONS
4.3. SIMULATION PROGRAM DELPHIN 6.1
reaches the VTT mould index of 6 after approx. a year, see figure 4.10. Thereby concluding that
there is a risk of mould growth. Another render should be considered.
The risk of mould growth on the internal surface is assessed by examining the figure 4.8. Here
limit for mould growth is determined by the isopleth graph for use in mould risk prediction by
Sedlbauer [50]. The threshold value is not exceeded for the internal surface of the hempcrete wall.
A significant difference in wall moisture is observed when driving rain is examined through a sim-
ulation of Danish weather with and without rain, see simulation in appendix L. The performance
of the wall simulation without rain data was markedly better. The wall never reached 100 % RH,
and the moisture content was markedly lower, with no problem with wet exterior render. Finally,
no moisture accumulation occurred. Therefore, it is assessed that simulating with all moisture
parameters possible is decisive for the outcome of a hygroscopic wall by hempcrete. A calculation
by the Glaser method cannot stand alone. A consideration for building with hempcrete would be
the performance of the external plaster, not letting in too much rain. Also, eaves or other initia-
tives to prevent the driving rain from reaching the wall would be relevant. The west and south
simulations performed the worst because of the driving rain coming South-West in Denmark. The
temperature profile of the wall seems good, leaving the minimum internal surface of a temperature
of 19.5 °C in the winter.
The simulations yielded striking results during the parameter analysis of the different capillary
saturation values. The walls with k-values of 0.073 and 0.16 exhibited significant moisture accu-
mulation over five years. Furthermore, the RH was also significantly high for both cases. Reaching
an RH of 100% in the depth of 0.2 meters into the construction and nearly throughout the con-
struction for the 0.073 and 0.16 k-values, respectively. Mould growth on the internal surfaces did
not occur but was at the limit of the isopleth graph [50]. When assessing these simulations with
higher, both walls are rejected while not complying with the BR18. Damage to the construction
might occur with a wet wall. The worse performance is present because the capillary suction speed
is increased. Thereby when exposed to water the hempcrete has faster suction properties, and this
determines that this current construction of the wall will be rejected. However, the decisive factor
is not necessarily the capillary saturation value (k-value) for hempcrete but rather the hygroscopic
properties of the exterior render. The render soaks too much water and lets in too much water to
the inner layer of hempcrete. It should act as a rain screen for the hempcrete.
In appendix L, a simulation of the thermal conductivity is displayed. Here there is little loss of
thermal capacity over the years in the outer part of the wall due to the accumulation of moisture.
Page 87 of 186

Comparative analysis
In the following chapter, the assessment of the hygrothermal properties of hempcrete, obtained by
experiments of this report, is compared with other materials, including insulation materials. This is
done to understand how it differentiates from other materials and clarify if it deviates significantly
from traditional insulation properties. This is used to help analyse and answer hypothesis 1.

Table 5.1: Obtained values and reference values for resistance number, M and capillary number,
k, from LBM1 method
In table 5.1, the capillary number for hempcrete is as many other materials, and the resistance
number is low, indicating that hempcrete will have water suction with a low resistance to the
water. It was seen in the literature that the k number of this study compared with others might be
a factor of five too low. If the high number is applicable, it can be suggested that there could be
a significant fast capillary transport of moisture in hempcrete when exposed to driving rain. This
aspect was investigated by simulations in Delphin 6.1, resulting in a clear negative output. When
increasing the k number, the wall did not comply with the regulation. Compared to other materials
such as glass wool and perlite, the k number of hempcrete is quite high. This does not dismiss
hempcrete; however, measures should be taken to ensure that the material is not directly exposed
to water if it is to be used in construction.

Table 5.2: Comparison of vapour permeability and water vapour resistance for different materials
In table 5.2, the permeability is primarily lower than other insulation materials such as rock wool,
perlite, glass wool and aerated concrete. It can be observed that these materials also have a Z-value
below 3 GPa∗s∗m2/kg, falling under the category of vapour-permeable. Hempcrete’s properties
are advantageous, with lower permeability. Compared to the other insulation materials, hempcrete,
to a greater extent, restricts moisture movement. This can be desirable in scenarios where it is
essential to minimize moisture infiltration into the building envelope, prevent condensation, and
maintain a controlled indoor environment.

Table 5.3: Obtained value from climate chamber method and reference values of adsorptions and

In table 5.3, the adsorption and desorption moisture content for different materials are presented,
and overall, hempcrete has a higher moisture content at different RHs. Another result is the pa-
rameter of hysteresis. The carbonated hempcrete, and especially the uncarbonated hempcrete, has
a higher hysteresis, and this is a point of criticism. In construction, materials with low or minimal
hysteresis are preferred because it is less prone to absorb and retain moisture. This is a factor
which, over time, may result in problems including moisture buildup, condensation, mould devel-
Page 89 of 186

opment, and deterioration. Again, this is not dismissing the material, but it requires considerations
regarding the choice of other building components in interaction with hempcrete.
Thermal conductivity

[1]
