2.8
Table 5.4: Obtained value from GHP experiment and reference values of thermal conductivity.
In table 5.4, the hempcrete has a thermal conductivity between mineral wool and aerated concrete.
However, aerated concrete is never solely the insulation component in a construction element. The
value signifies that hempcrete is an insulation material, though not as good as mineral wool and
most insulation materials. To achieve the same U-value for construction, the quantity of hempcrete
compared to traditional insulation would have to be almost twice as high. This is a disadvantage
leaving thicker construction of the building envelope, though is it resulting in a great thermal mass.
The thermal mass acts as a heat accumulator and is also an energy-optimizing parameter of the
building.
The material properties investigated in this report do not significantly differ from traditional in-
sulation materials. Therefore, it is estimated that hempcrete can be used as insulation material
without any immediate issues. Hypothesis number 1 is thereby partially accepted regarding ther-
mal conductivity, sorption, permeability, and capillary suction corresponding with already used
insulation material.
Page 90 of 186
==PAGE:109==
6
Discussion
6.1
Test specimens
In the following section, the implications that emerged during the execution and completion of
the test specimens will be briefly highlighted. Overall there is a distinct lack of standardisation
about fabrication, moulding and curing methodologies. This might have influenced the experi-
ments. The greatest deviation observed was the density between the different sample types, which
depends on the moulding process, where a more compacted wet hempcrete mix would result in
a higher density. However, isolated for each experiment, there was no significant variation in the
results, even though the samples were cast at different times. It can be assessed that the method
used to produce the samples is useful. However, for future research, a thorough procedure could
be investigated.
The carbonation process of the test specimens did not complete, and experiments were conducted
based on uncarbonated hempcrete. It is not recommended to make a primitive CO2 chamber. The
conditions should be more controlled by PID, and the calibration limits for the CO2 logger should
reach higher than 10,000 ppm. Overall, it is assumed that the main source of error is caused by too
high RH and too low CO2 in periods. The percentage of Calcium Dihydroxide in the Batichan-
vre product was 10-20 %. This affects the hempcrete, but the ratio is not significant. Thereby,
the experiments might deliver reasonable results. A comparison of uncarbonated and carbonated
specimens was conducted for some of the experiments, and the outcome was as expected. Ob-
tained value suggested a higher water vapour permeability and higher binding of water molecules
to the inner surfaces for the carbonated specimens. The overall output of the carbonation of lime
is that the properties slightly change. A wall, roof or floor would take many years to carbonate.
Thereby, the numbers of the experiments both represent the hempcrete construction and apper-
taining properties over a lifetime. Over time, the permeability and sorption will increase, but not
significantly.
6.2
Hempcrete as an alternative
When comparing the alternative insulation material, hempcrete, with traditional insulation mate-
rial such as mineral wool, differences occur regarding hygrothermal properties. The hempcrete
is overall having a higher capillary suction of 0.033 kg/m2 · s0.5 and higher sorption curve at
adsorption 1.3 % and 9.7 % at respectively 30 and 94 % RH. The desorption of 6.2 % at 30 %
91
==PAGE:110==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.3. IMPLEMENTATION IN DANISH INDUSTRY
RH indicated the high hysteresis of the product. It decreases when carbonating. This alternative
insulation material is thereby better at having a higher moisture content and suction speed of the
water. In comparing water vapour permeability, the 2.5 10−11
kg
m∗s∗Pa for the dry experiment were
primarily lower than other insulation materials, indicating that hempcrete is less prone to allow
water vapour penetration. When examining the thermal conductivity, hempcrete performs worse,
having a value of 0.08 W/(m*k), compared to mineral wool. Hypothesis one stated that hempcrete
had different moisture properties than traditional insulation materials and is clarified above. It
could be an alternative insulation material, while properties correspond to other materials’ with
different properties. Hypothesis number 1 is thereby partially accepted, but implementation in the
industry is evaluated in the next section 6.3.
6.3
Implementation in Danish industry
U-value calculations
To determine if hempcrete is a useful insulation material with useful hygrothermal properties able
to implement in the Danish construction industry, calculations and simulations were conducted.
The resulting U-value fulfils the requirement set by the BR18 of 0.30 W/(m2 ∗K), when having a
U-value of 0.189 W/(m2 ∗K) by a 400 mm thick hempcrete insulation in the wall, see section 4.1
Thermal calculations. Thermal bridges are minimal since complete insulation surrounds all joints
when casting in situ. If the hempcrete is not applied to all parts of the building, the existence of
possible thermal bridges would be interesting to determine. Another advantage of the material is
its great thermal mass, which regulates the temperature, resulting in less energy consumption.
Calcultions by Glaser method
To evaluate if the hempcrete is applicable regarding moisture performance, the rough estimate by
the Glaser method in section 4.2.1 indicated a hempcrete wall without moisture problems, usable
in construction. The results displayed a wall with no condensation, while a RH above 100 %
didn’t occur. However, in the winter months, the RH were dangerously high, respectively 94.4
to 99.6 % in between external render and hempcrete. An extreme calculation with the worst-case
climate scenario did result in condensation in the hempcrete layer. The method cannot stand alone
to evaluate the moisture performance, while it has limitations as stationary conditions without
parameters such as rain and radiation for the weather profile. Furthermore, the method does not
consider moisture transport via capillarity or sorption, which is crucial to implement when con-
sidering a material prone to moisture transport.
Simulations by Delphin 6.1
Delphin 6.1 simulations and associated results in section 4.3 was used to evaluate if hempcrete is
useful as an alternative insulation material in a wall. The dynamic 1D simulation has the most re-
alistic weather data possible, and all aspects of moisture transport in the material are implemented.
In the following, the results for the hempcrete wall simulated in Delphin 6.1 are shown.
Page 92 of 186
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.3. IMPLEMENTATION IN DANISH INDUSTRY
Risk of moisture
accumulation:
Results showed that the wall didn’t dry out during summer but,
on the contrary, accumulated moisture occurred over the years ap-
prox. 2 kg/m2 pr. year. When the wall does not stabilize over
time, the evaluation criteria are not complied with.
Risk of mould growth
on surfaces:
No risk of mould growth occurred valid for internal surfaces. The
risk mould growth at VTT index of 6 occurred for the facade, sim-
ulating 100 % coverage of mould on the facade.
Risk of condensation:
Results displayed a RH above 80 % in the first half of the wall and
100 % in the exterior render, decreasing into the wall construction.
This free saturation condition of approx. 100 % occurs, and great
amounts of water are transported. The exterior render is wet most
of the year with 200 and 500 kg/m3. This is critical and can re-
sult in long-term problems with moisture and risk of condensation
occur in the outer layer.
Risk of deterioration of
construction:
With the high RH in the wall, the mass percentage is potentially
at 12 % in the hempcrete and likely higher in the structural wood.
This is due to the high RH inside the construction present for long
periods, indicating a risk of deterioration of the structural wood.
The condition is not directly favourable for mould growth, but an
initiative to prevent the risk of wood deterioration should be taken.
It could be positioning the wood closer to the inner surface.
Effect of driving rain:
The simulations with and without rain displayed a notable differ-
ence. When no rain is present in the simulation the moisture per-
formance of the wall was good, with no risk for moisture accumu-
lation, mould growth and risk of deterioration of the construction.
The effects of driving rain are concluded to be high.
Risk of loss of thermal
insulation capacity:
A slight decrease of thermal capacity is observed in the outer part
of the wall.
The performance of the wall does not meet all criteria, and the evaluation of the wall with current
materials is not suitable for construction, according to BR18. The parameter analysis of the capil-
lary suction property in hempcrete confirmed this, while the performance with a higher and more
realistic k number performed unacceptably. Here moisture accumulation became critical, and the
risk of mould growth likewise, amongst others.
Initial moisture content is another problem when knowing that the hempcrete wall has a dry-out
period, where water will evaporate. The wall should thereby not be built in situ in the winter
Page 93 of 186
==PAGE:112==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.4. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT OF HEMPCRETE
season because of the risk of build up moisture content in the wall [51]. Another parameter is the
pH value of the hempcrete, which prevents fungus and mould growth. A consideration could be
that a hempcrete wall can have a higher moisture content than normal wall constructions because
it is protected through the chemical component. However, when carbonation occurs, the pH value
decreases, and ultimately the pH should become neutral, but this is still questioned today if it ever
reaches this point, see section Carbonation 3.2. Over time, see sections Water vapour permeability
3.5 and Capillarity 3.4, it was also observed that moisture properties for hempcrete change, result-
ing in potentially even better moisture transport.
The simulated hempcrete wall does not have a convincing performance. It only performs well in a
few parameters, such as no risk of mould growth on the inner surface, an overall mass% below 20,
and almost maintaining the thermal capacity. To minimize the moisture issues of the wall, some
interpretations of the simulations are conducted. It was evident that the rain had a problematic
effect on the poor performance of the wall. If the hempcrete can be properly protected by the rain,
it has full potential as an alternative insulation material. Initiatives that can optimize the wall to
enable the use of hempcrete are highlighted below:
• Firstly, it seems that the moisture content between the exterior render and the hempcrete is
build up. A possible solution could be the creation of cohesion between the materials to
prevent moisture accumulation between them. The material properties of the exterior render
should be able to protect against rain and be permeable. If it cannot fully protect against
rain and absorbs some, it should be able to dry out fast and not contain the rain for long. To
accommodate a solution of proper exterior render, properties such as thermal conductivity,
permeability and porosity are important. The thermal conductivity should be high because
the increased heat transfer helps evaporation. The vapour permeability should be present to
enable moisture to escape to the exterior. The open structure of a porous material is good
when it allows airflow and enables trapped moisture to escape.
• Another solution to the issue could be a rain screen. This could consist of an externally
ventilated facade that repels all rain. The facade cladding could consist of wood and venti-
lated space between the facade cladding and hempcrete to prevent further moisture issues.
The protection of rain could too be supported by eaves on the roof. However, adding a rain
screen in the form of facade cladding would be a solution that deviates from the simple con-
cept, which is one of the reasons for using hempcrete in the first place. Also, construction
has to be redesigned to attach the wood cladding to the wall. Hempcrete is not a material
for mounting. It will break if big holes are drilled into the material.
6.4
Environmental impact of hempcrete
To understand if a product is environmentally sustainable, a life cycle assessment is carried out
in the program OpenLCA, see chapter 2 Life Cycle Assessment. The LCA enlightened that the
production stage, A1-A3, is the largest contributor to the overall total performance except for two
categories. The second largest contributor is phase A4, the transport of manufactured products
from France to Denmark. The net positive results for GHG emissions were 4.58E-01 kg CO2-
Page 94 of 186
==PAGE:113==
CHAPTER 6. DISCUSSION
6.4. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT OF HEMPCRETE
Eq, concluding that hempcrete is a useful material because it minimizes the environmental carbon
footprint compared to traditional insulation materials such as stone wool and aerated concrete. The
product is useful nowadays as requirements are only imposed on climate change impact, according
to BR18. When hempcrete was compared for all life cycle stages using the ReCipe Midpoint (H)
method, it performed worse in 11 out of 18 categories. This raises considerations about whether
the current requirements are inadequate. In the future, it should be considered whether a more
holistic comparative basis is more relevant for a sustainable future.
In general, the fact that hempcrete is not locally produced reduces the performance of the output
in the LCA. If an extrapolation of the future transport industry was considered to run on green
electricity, the transport parameter could be counted as a smaller proportion of the output. This
would give hempcrete an even better performance than stone wool and aerated concrete in sev-
eral impact categories. Further, it should be discussed whether the carbonation of lime should be
included in LCA in the future. In reality, the sequestration of CO2 is because of another process
under production where the CO2 was released to create a buildable useful lime for the industry.
This should be considered a zero-sum game instead and not impact the calculations.
Page 95 of 186
==PAGE:114==
7
Conclusion
To accommodate the significant challenges of global warming and reach the European Green Deal,
future sustainable materials are needed. The goal of this project was to investigate an alternative
insulation material usable in Danish construction concerning environmental impact and hygrother-
mal performance. To reach this goal, experimental work, simulations, and calculations determined
the hygrothermal properties of hempcrete. This work showed the hygrothermal performance. The
assessment of the environmental impact was determined by creating an LCA and comparing it to
traditional insulation.
Hempcrete is considered a promising environmentally sustainable material, being useful in the
Danish construction industry. This is because the impact of GHG emissions is lower than tradi-
tional materials, thereby minimizing the carbon footprint.
Hempcrete has hygrothermal properties assessed to be applicable as insulation material compared
to nowadays insulation materials. When calculating the thermal performance of the wall, compli-
ance with BR18 was obliged. However, when simulated in Delphin 6.1, results indicated moisture
problems due to a lack of proper rain protection. If the hempcrete wall is protected by rain, it
has full potential as an alternative insulation material. Though, if the wall is conducted as the
project example, the hempcrete wall did not oblige the BR18 demands. With reservations that
there is a rain screen solution available, either in the form of improved renders or facade cladding,
hempcrete could be used as an alternative insulation material in the Danish construction industry.
Future investigations
Several aspects arose in the research progress of this master thesis. First of all, it would be in-
teresting to measure real-life hygrothermal behaviour. This aspect was initially also a subject of
the thesis in the early stages, but it was not possible due to a lack of funds. Also, the ongoing
moisture problem of the wall would be interesting to address and investigate proper lime render
for construction and the perfect property combination. As to laboratory experiments, it would be
interesting to investigate the effects of carbonation. In continuation of this topic, the consequences
of having a wall with neutral pH could be determined. The thermal performance of hempcrete
in different relative humidities would too be interesting to investigate. Concerning the thermal
performance of thermal bridges, it would be interesting to conduct simulations. Furthermore,
simulations in 2D would be of interest for hygrothermal modelling.

B
Life cycle assessment
B.1
Bill of materials
Figure B.1: Bill of materials LCA of hempcrete
Data collection of traditional insulation materials for comparison:
Stone wool:
Stone wool found in the ecoinvent database, it is produced in DK, transport is considered only
30 km. End of life is considered landfill. Thermal conductivity of 0.037, thereby a factor 0.45 in
OpenLCA in comparison.
Autoclaved aerated concrete:
Aerated concrete found in the ecoinvent database used, it is produced in DK, transport is consid-
ered only 30 km. Ends up in landfills and has a thermal conductivity of 0.1, thereby a factor of
1.25 in OpenLCA in comparison.

==PAGE:137==
D
Instructions for casting of
hempcrete
This guide gives the inexperienced user a quick insight into how to cast hemp concrete. The
guide makes it possible to work with hemp concrete quickly and easily without too much prior
knowledge of hemp concrete.
D.1
Goal
The purpose of casting hempcrete in small moulds is to be able to use the samples for later use in
the laboratory. At the same time, this guide is designed to create a standardized way as possible
of casting hempcrete.
D.2
Equipment
Materials
• Hempshives from BAFA GmbH
• Batichanvre lime NHL-3.5 from Saint-Astier
• Water from the hose
General equipment
• Weight – precision of 0,00 g
• Vernier caliper – precision of 0,00 mm
• Stirrer
• Box for equipment
• Box for waste (the hempcrete must harden before disposal)
• Roller table for materials, moulds and samples of hempcrete
• Duct tape
• Pen and paper
• Rubber scraper
• Measuring cup
• Bowls
• Big plastic bags
• Molds
119
==PAGE:138==
APPENDIX D. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CASTING OF HEMPCRETE
D.3. SAFETY
Safety equipment
• Gloves: Nitrilgummi > 0,2 mm breakthrough time > 480 minutes
• Clothed: wear a laboratory coat and clothes that cover all skin and protect against solid
chemicals.
• Safety glasses: wear safety glasses with side protection
• Respirator: wear respirator type S/SL
• Footwear: safety shoes
License to lab and machines:
• Take the safety quiz on DTU inside under “Arbejdsmiljø og Sikkerhed på BYG” and pass it
• Get the green access label on the student card and access to the lab by student card by
contacting the reception in the building 118 at DTU
• Fill in a chemical risk assessment and get it approved by a professor assigned to the labora-
tory of mortar and afterwards get it signed by your supervisor
• Get an introduction by a professor of the laboratory of mortar in building 130 at DTU
• The professor giving introduction sets you up in the EIS system. This system is used to
book the stirring machine in the lab.
D.3
Safety
There are several safety measures which are important to note when working with hempcrete, es-
pecially the lime; Batichanvre NHL-3,5. Batichanvre NHL-3,5 is a chemical being dangerous if
not careful when handling it, especially in free form when it is dusty. This chemical is the reason
for wearing all safety equipment.
Batichanvre does not have acute toxicity concerning oral, skin, or respiratory exposure. The sub-
stance is classified as an irritant for the skin and respiratory ways and presents a risk of serious
eye damage. No deadly effects are suspected; the principal danger is restricted to localized effects.
(effects pH)
If anything happens when working with the lime, do as follows*(fodnote: ref til safetyblad):
IF INHALED: Remove person to fresh air and keep comfortable for breathing. Immediately call
a doctor.
IF SWALLOWED: Immediately call a doctor. DO NOT INDUCE VOMITING. NEVER give
anything by mouth if the victim is rapidly losing consciousness or is unconscious or convulsing.
Rinse mouth thoroughly with water. Have the victim drink two glasses of water. If vomiting oc-
curs naturally, have the victim lean forward to reduce the risk of aspiration.
IF ON SKIN (or hair): Take off immediately all contaminated clothing. Rinse skin with water.
(15-20 minutes).
IF IN EYES: Rinse cautiously with water for several minutes. (15-20 minutes). Remove contact
lenses, if present and easy to do. Continue rinsing. Immediately call a doctor.
D.4
Method
The hempcrete is when mixed consisting of hemp shives, lime and water. When hardening, the
water evaporates, and thereby, the final mix consists of hemp and lime. The lime needs air and
Page 120 of 186
==PAGE:139==
APPENDIX D. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CASTING OF HEMPCRETE
D.5. PROCEDURE
water [41] to harden because it is natural hydraulic lime. The best hardening and carbonation con-
ditions for hempcrete is 60-65 RH and 20 degree Celsius. If a CO2 climate chamber is available,
then it is preferable to use to fasten the carbonation process.
Mix when casting hempcrete:
16,6 % hemp shives
33,3 % lime
50 % water
D.5
Procedure
Mixing procedure
1) All safety equipment is put on. The most dangerous phase occurs when handling the lime in the
free form before and under mixing. Each bowl in the stirring machine can contain ¼ of one cubic
meter of hempcrete mix.
2) Weigh out 500,00 g. of lime in the mixing bowl; it is important to avoid raising lime dust.
Therefore, handle it with care. Use a spoon and pour the lime carefully into the bowl close to the
bottom of the bowl.
3) Weigh out 750,00 g. of water in the measuring cup.
4) Pour the water into the bowl with lime and mix it slowly with the rubber scraper.
5) Place the bowl in the stirrer and turn on level I. Ensure the water and lime are fully stirred so
no lumps are present.
6) Weigh out 250,00 g. of hemp shives and slowly pour it into the bowl in the stirrer.
7) Stir the mix in 10 minutes.
8) Snowball test: Take a handful of hempcrete in your hand, and squeeze the hempcrete in between
your hands 3 times. Throw the hempcrete ball into the air; if it does not fall apart, it is usable.
Casting in moulds procedure
1) Molds are oiled to prevent the hempcrete from getting stuck in the moulds. Disperse the oil
with paper.
2) Pour some hempcrete into the mould, and push some of the hempcrete into each corner to make
sure that no holes will occur in the finished samples.
3) Pour and spread the hempcrete into the mould, do not press but gently use your hands to spread
the hempcrete.
4) When the mould is filled with hempcrete, take a plate 100 mm x 100 mm and put a load of 2 kg
on top. Press the surface with the 100 mm x 100 mm plate with two bricks on top.
5) Weight the samples in the moulds
6) After 36 to 48 hours free the sample from the molds gently by screwing the molds apart.
7) Weight the sample without the moulds
8) Place the samples in a climate chamber with 60-65 RH and 20 Co
9) Let the samples harden and carbonize the next 1-3 months. Weight them regularly to track the
process.
10) Have a dummy sample and regularly test if the carbonation is done by adding thymolpthalein
indicator. If a blue colour occurs, carbonation is not done.
Page 121 of 186
==PAGE:140==
APPENDIX D. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CASTING OF HEMPCRETE
D.5. PROCEDURE

APPENDIX D. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CASTING OF HEMPCRETE

E
Instructions building CO2 chamber
E.1
Goal
To enhance the carbonation process of the air lime in the hempcrete samples.
E.2
Equipment
Materials
• CO2 pump
• CO2 refill
• MagnesiumNitrat
• CO2 bottle UN1956
General equipment
• Rack for test specimens
• Big petri dish ø20cm
• Solid plastic
• Mini fan
Measuring equipment
• HOBO CO2 logger
• HOBO RH logger
• HOBO Temp. logger
124
==PAGE:143==
APPENDIX E. INSTRUCTIONS BUILDING CO2 CHAMBER
E.3. SAFETY
Figure E.1: Mini fan in the chamber
Figure E.2: HOBO CO2, Temp and RH logger
E.3
Safety
The occupational exposure concentration limit of CO2 is 5000 ppm according to the safety data
sheet of UN1956. This was complied with in the experiment, and also the concentration was high
in the chamber, not outside the CO2 chamber.
E.4
Method
To enhance the carbonation process of the lime in the hempcrete samples a CO2 chamber is built.
In the ambient air, a concentration of 400 ppm is present and as long as CO2 is present the car-
bonation process will occur. This process is time-consuming but exposure to a high concentration
of CO2 along with other constant conditions will enhance the process. According to [35] the
carbonation process of lime is optimal if there is a high CO2 concentration above 2000 ppm., a
Page 125 of 186
==PAGE:144==
APPENDIX E. INSTRUCTIONS BUILDING CO2 CHAMBER
E.5. PROCEDURE
temperature of 20 deg. Celsius and relative humidity of about 50-60 %.
E.5
Procedure
1) Build the CO2 chamber by creating a membrane around the rack. Make an envelope of plastic
around the rack by fixing it with duct tape. Seal the box with duct tape everywhere a leakage might
be.
2) Calibrate the CO2 logger to zero and 5000 ppm.
3) Before closing the box totally install the loggers in the box, and make sure that it is possible to
take a reading when looking through the plastic into the box.
4) Place the magnesium nitrate in the bottom in a big petri dish and the mini fan too. This is to
ensure that the air gets circulated and thereby the salt concentration will affect the RH.
5) Slice a hole in the plastic and release CO2 into the chamber.
6) Observe the concentration of CO2 and check hereby if there is a leakage. If no leakage occurs
then place the test specimens in the rack.
7) Place the CO2 chamber in a room with a constant temperature of 20 deg. celsius, for instance in
a climate chamber for curing of concrete. 8) Observe and release more CO2 into the chamber with
test specimens. The concentration should be above 2000 ppm. Track the concentration to figure
out how fast the CO2 concentration is decreasing. See figure below, in one hour, the CO2 chamber
went from 5000 ppm to below 100 ppm. The need for supply is every 30 minutes manually.
The figure below shows the concentration when no test specimens are in the chamber.
Page 126 of 186
==PAGE:145==
APPENDIX E. INSTRUCTIONS BUILDING CO2 CHAMBER
E.5. PROCEDURE
Figure E.3: CO2 chamber conditions when no test specimens were inside the chamber. The graph
is for 24 hours and a illustrated a tight chamber
It was found out that the CO2 in the CO2 pump was of too small amount. A bottle of 20 liters
UN1956 was put into operation instead. Down below in figure E.5 the experiment of carbonation
is illustrated.
Figure E.4: Open CO2 chamber to identify all elements

I
Instructions for Cup method
I.1
Procedure of prepping
To conduct the cup test, the specimens are prepared by sealing the sides of each specimen with
plastic rings measuring ø100. Epoxy is used as the sealing material and will be placed between the
specimen edge and the ø100 ring. This sealing aims to prevent vapor diffusion through the sides
of the specimen, effectively restricting vapor diffusion to the designated surface area. The whole
procedure of prepping the specimens is elaborated:
140
==PAGE:159==
5.
Apply sealant to the sides to prevent epoxy from 
entering the test specimens. 
 
 
6.
Press the sealant into the specimens and firmly cover 
the sides with aluminum foil. 
 
 
 
 
7.
Trim the aluminum foil to fit the test specimens.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
8.
Place the test specimens centrally within the rings.
 
 
 
APPENDIX I. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CUP METHOD
I.1. PROCEDURE OF PREPPING
Page 141 of 186
==PAGE:160==
9.
Fill the space between the test specimens and the rings with epoxy.  
10. After the epoxy has cured for 3 days, carefully remove the aluminum on the surface of the test specimen. 
 
 
 
Prepping of test specimens in cup equipment.  
The procedure is elaborated below for respectively dry and wet cup. 
Cup method procedure: 
1.
Find cup equipment, top and bottom  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2.
Fasten the glass cup with rubber pieces
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
APPENDIX I. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CUP METHOD
I.1. PROCEDURE OF PREPPING
Page 142 of 186
==PAGE:161==
3.
Put on the black rubber ring 
 
 
 
 
4.
Place the metal grate on top of glass cup 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
5.
Place specimen on top, make sure the rubber ring is 
clean to ensure tightness 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6.
Place a black rubber ring on the edge of the specimen 
 
 
 
APPENDIX I. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CUP METHOD
I.1. PROCEDURE OF PREPPING
Page 143 of 186
==PAGE:162==
7.
Place the white plastic ring on top of the rubber ring 
 
 
 
 
 
8.
Screw the top lid on 
 
 
 
9.
Fasten the top and bottom of the cup equipment
 
 
 
 
10. The cups are placed in the climate chamber
 
 
 
APPENDIX I. INSTRUCTIONS FOR CUP METHOD
I.1. PROCEDURE OF PREPPING
Page 144 of 186
==PAGE:163==
11. Be careful when weighting the specimens, place both 
hands in each entrance hole, “screw” the cups into 
the holes in the circle. 
 
 
 
12. Dry cup method is with the same procedure. Desiccant 
is silica gel and step 4 is not conducted.

==PAGE:174==
J
Instructions for settings of GHP
This guide gives the inexperienced user a quick insight into using the Guarded Hot Plate at DTU
in building 119. In this instruction, there will be a description of the method and equipment and
also the results. This guide builds on theory from the European Standard EN 12664 [EN-12664].
J.1
Goal
A guarded hot plate apparatus is used to evaluate a material’s thermal conductivity describing the
ability of the material to transfer heat. The lambda value is determined by this experiment having
the unit (W/mK).
J.2
Equipment for GHP
• Test specimens 300 mm x 300 mm x 30 mm
• Record machine in building 119/basement at DTU
• Single thermocouple
• Reading instrument for temperatures
• Plate apparatus with already connecting voltmeter, printer, therpcouples,
• Voltmeter
• Thermocouples
• Cooling bath
• BenchLink Data Logger
• Computer with BenchLink program
• Screwdriver for installation of wires in the logger
J.3
Structure and theoretical basis
The basic idea behind the guarded hot plate experiment is to induce a one-dimensional heat flow in
the material. This is the primary principle of the guarded hot plate experiment, and the apparatus
is built according to the standard DS/EN 12664 [5].
J.4
Procedure
1) Start the cooling bath. It can take several hours.
1.1 Turn on the cold hoes with water on the wall, set it on 230
156
==PAGE:175==
APPENDIX J. INSTRUCTIONS FOR SETTINGS OF GHP
J.4. PROCEDURE
1.2 Turn on the HETO
1.3 Measure the temperature of the water in the cooling bath, preferably it should reach 12.5 oC
Figure J.1: Cooling bath with Heto
2) Setup of the BenchLink Data Logger system
2.1 Pull out a disc from the backside of the logger and connect the thermocouples from the cold
and hot sides in the first channel
2.2 Connect the wires from the voltmeter in the second channel of the logger
2.3 Insert the disc in the Data logger
2.4 Connect the computer, computer screen, mouse, keyboard and Data logger by cables and add
power
2.5 Setup of the logging system at the computer, follow pictures below:
Page 157 of 186
==PAGE:176==
Setup of the logging system at the computer in Benchlink. Open Benchlink on the computer. 
1. Click on the first menu “Configure Instruments”. 
2. Click on “Remove Instruments” and “Delete Data Log”. 
3. Add instruments and choose channels 101 and 102. 
 
4. Click on second menu: “Configure Channels”,  
5. Mark the channels 101 and 102 name them. 
 
 
APPENDIX J. INSTRUCTIONS FOR SETTINGS OF GHP
J.4. PROCEDURE
Page 158 of 186
==PAGE:177==
6. Click on the menu “Scan and Log Data” 
7. Click on Immediately to set the intervals of time between scans 
 
8. Click on “<Date><Time>” and select a place to store data on the PC 
 
APPENDIX J. INSTRUCTIONS FOR SETTINGS OF GHP
J.4. PROCEDURE
Page 159 of 186
==PAGE:178==
9. Click on “start” to start the simulation.  
10. Click on “Quick Graph” to follow logging.
